Dialectic -- Jadal
intermediate
8 min
Analyzing the Jadal Exchange on Marriage During 'Iddah
Historical jadal example on marriage during iddah with objection and answer

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the mudda'i, mu'tarid, and jawab in a jadal exchange and explain how the retraction of a Companion's ruling functions in dialectical argumentation.

Core Idea
Jadal resolves apparent contradictions by tracing authorities' final positions.

This passage from the usul al-fiqh tradition presents a real historical debate between the Malikis and the majority over a ruling on marriage during 'iddah. By studying this exchange, you will learn how structured objection (i'tirad) and answer (jawab) work in jadal, and how an apparent conflict between Companions' rulings is reconciled by establishing a retraction.

Guided Path
1
Identify the mudda'i and his ruling
The claimant (mudda'i) is Ali ibn Abi Talib. His ruling: separate the couple, require her to complete the first 'iddah then observe a second 'iddah from the second marriage, and give her the choice to marry the second man after that.
This is the position the majority defends.
2
Identify the mu'tarid and their objection
The objectors (mu'tarid) are the Malikis. They cite a narration from Umar ibn al-Khattab ruling that the woman is permanently prohibited from marrying the second man if consummation occurred.
This objection challenges the validity of Ali's ruling by presenting a conflicting Companion precedent.
3
Analyze the jawab
The majority responds by arguing that Umar later retracted his ruling upon hearing Ali's reasoning. The retraction is established through a narration where Ali publicly corrected Umar, and Umar accepted the correction.
In jadal, a retraction cancels the authoritative force of the earlier ruling.
4
Evaluate the strength of the answer
The retraction is a valid answer because it shows that the authoritative source (Umar) ultimately agreed with Ali, removing the contradiction. The majority relies on the principle that the later ruling of a Companion abrogates the earlier one.
This method preserves harmony among the Companions' rulings and upholds the Sunnah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أخبرنا عبد المجيد عن ابن جريج قال أخبرنا عطاء أن رجلا طلق امرأته، فاعتدت منه حتى إذا بقي شيء من عدتها نكحها رجل في آخر عدتها – جهلا وبنى بها، فأتي علي بن أبي طالب رضي الله عنه في ذلك، ففرق بينهما، وأمرها أن تعتد ما بقي من عدتها الأولى ثم تعتد من هذا عدة مستقبلة، فإذا انقضت عدتها فهي بالخيار إن شاءت نكحت وإن شاءت فلا. فيعترض عليهم المالكية بما نُقل عن عمر بن الخطاب رضي الله عنه أنه حكم بتأبيد تحريمها عليه فقد روى مالك في الموطأ عن ابن شهاب الزهري عن سعيد بن المسيب وسليمان بن يسار أن عمر بن الخطاب رضي الله عنه فرق بين طليحة الأسدية وزوجها رشيد الثقفي لما تزوجها في العدة من شخص آخر، وقال: «أيما امرأة نكحت في عدتها فإن كان زوجها الذي تزوجها لم يدخل بها فرق بينهما ثم اعتدت بقية عدتها من الأول ثم كان الآخر خاطبا من الخطاب، وإن كان دخل بها فرق بينهما ثم اعتدت بقية عدتها من الأول ثم اعتدت من الآخر، ثم لا يجتمعان أبدا». وقد أجاب الجمهور على هذا الاعتراض بأنه ثبت رجوع عمر رضي الله عنه إلى قول علي رضي الله عنه، وذلك في الحادثة المذكورة نفسها، فقد روي أن امرأة من قريش قد تزوجها رجل من ثقيف في عدتها، فلما بلغ عمر ذلك أرسل إليهما ففرق بينهما وعاقبهما، وقال: لا ينكحها أبدا، وجعل الصداق في بيت المال، وفشا ذلك بين الناس، فبلغ عليا، فقال: «رحم الله أمير المؤمنين، ما بال الصداق وبيت المال ؛ إنهما جهلا فينبغي للإمام أن يردهما إلى السنة»؛ قيل : فما تقول أنت فيهما ؟ قال : «لها الصداق بما استحل من فرجها ويفرق بينهما ولا جلد عليهما وتكمل عدتها من الأول، ثم تكمل العدة من الآخر، ثم يكون خاطبا»، فقال: «أيها الناس ردوا الجهالات إلى السنة».
Abd al-Majid narrated to us from Ibn Jurayj, who said: Ata' narrated to us that a man divorced his wife. She observed her waiting period (iddah) until only a portion remained. Then a man married her during the remainder of her iddah out of ignorance and consummated the marriage. The case was brought to Ali ibn Abi Talib, may Allah be pleased with him, who separated them, ordered her to complete the remainder of the first iddah, then observe a new iddah from this marriage, and after its completion she had the choice to marry the second man or not. The Malikis objected to this using the narration from Umar ibn al-Khattab, may Allah be pleased with him, that he ruled in the case of Tulayha al-Asadiyya and her husband Rashid al-Thaqafi, who married her during her iddah from another man, that the marriage was prohibited forever. Malik narrated in the Muwatta' from Ibn Shihab al-Zuhri from Sa'id ibn al-Musayyab and Sulayman ibn Yasar that Umar separated them and said: 'Any woman who marries during her iddah, if her husband has not consummated the marriage, they are separated, she completes the remainder of the first iddah, and the second man becomes a suitor. If he consummated, they are separated, she completes the remainder of the first iddah, then observes a second iddah from the second, and they will never be reunited.' The majority (of scholars) responded to this objection by establishing that Umar, may Allah be pleased with him, retracted to Ali's opinion, may Allah be pleased with him, in the same incident. It is narrated that a woman from Quraysh married a man from Thaqif during her iddah. When Umar learned of this, he sent for them, separated them, punished them, said they would never marry, and placed the dowry in the public treasury. This became widely known among the people, reaching Ali, who said: 'May Allah have mercy on the Commander of the Faithful. What has the dowry to do with the public treasury? They were ignorant; the Imam should return them to the Sunnah.' It was asked: 'What do you say about them?' He said: 'She gets the dowry for what was made lawful of her private parts, they are separated, no flogging is due, she completes her iddah from the first, then completes the iddah from the second, then he becomes a suitor.' Then he said: 'O people, return the ignorances to the Sunnah.'
Source pages p. 479

Commentary
This passage presents a historical example of jadal (dialectical disputation) in legal methodology. The Malikis raise an objection by citing a precedent from Umar that seems to contradict Ali's ruling. The majority's response is to show that Umar later retracted his opinion upon hearing Ali's reasoning. This demonstrates a key principle in jadal: objections are answered not by rejecting the source but by showing that the authority changed his view. The narrative illustrates how dialectic functions in usul al-fiqh: it is a structured method to reconcile apparent conflicts between rulings, ultimately returning to the Sunnah. The example also highlights the importance of tracing the final position of a Companion rather than an isolated statement.

Key Terms
العدة
الجدل
الاعتراض

Checkpoint
What is the main argument used by the majority to respond to the Malikis' objection in the story?
A) They claimed the Malikis had misquoted Umar.
B) They cited that Umar later retracted his opinion upon hearing Ali's reasoning.
C) They argued that Umar's ruling only applied to certain regions.
D) They denied the authenticity of Umar's ruling.

Key Takeaway
The majority's use of Umar's retraction demonstrates how jadal resolves apparent conflicts by appealing to the final, corrected position of an authority.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Find another jadal example from usul al-fiqh (e.g., the debate on qiyas when a text is present). Identify the mudda'i, mu'tarid, and jawab, and explain the logical move used to answer the objection.
?
Explain why retraction is a valid move in jadal. Find another instance where a Companion retracted a ruling (e.g., Umar's retraction on the inheritance of a divorced woman during 'iddah) and describe how it functions dialectically.

Dialectic -- Jadal
intermediate
15 min
Objection to Istishab by Mutual Opposition
Defines istishab, states its acceptance, then explains first objection type: mutual opposition

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify a mu'arada bi al-mithl objection in a juristic debate and evaluate how the claimant responds by specifying the scope of liability.

Core Idea
Mutual opposition turns istishab against itself; the claimant must delimit the agreed-upon occupation of liability.

Istishab (presumption of continuity) is a foundational principle in usul al-fiqh, but its use in argumentation is not uncontested. This passage first defines istishab and notes the disagreement over its validity. It then introduces the first type of dialectical objection against istishab: mutual opposition (al-mu'arada bi al-mithl), where the opponent counters with an equal presumption. We will examine how this objection works and how the proponent of istishab answers it.

Guided Path
1
Define istishab and its default ruling
Find the definition in the passage: 'remaining upon the original ruling'. The original ruling is either permissibility (ibaha) or innocence (bara'a). Understand that this default applies when no evidence is present.
2
Identify the two camps on istishab
The passage mentions that the majority of usul scholars accept istishab as a valid proof, while the Hanafis and Abu al-Husayn al-Basri reject it. This disagreement sets the stage for dialectical objections.
3
Recognize the nature of the objection
The objection of mutual opposition (mu'arada bi al-mithl) transforms a substantive dispute into a dialectical one. Instead of arguing the merits of the case, the opponent counters the claimant's istishab with another istishab that leads to a different ruling in the same matter.
This is a standard jadal technique.
4
Analyze the first example (killing unknowingly in dar al-harb)
The Shafi'i claims no blood money is due based on original innocence. The opponent objects: 'We all agree that the liability was occupied by the killing itself. So whoever claims that expiation clears it bears the burden.' The claimant answers by specifying that only the expiation is proven to occupy the liability, not the blood money.
5
Analyze the second example (intentional murderer and kaffarah)
The Maliki claims no expiation is due. The opponent (Shafi'i/Hanafi) objects: 'The liability was occupied by consensus upon killing. So whoever claims that paying the value clears it needs proof.' The answer is to limit the agreed occupation to the value only.

المسألة .
وذلك مثل أن يقول الشافعي في من قتل مسلمًا في دار الحرب وهو لا يعلم إسلامه : إنه لا يجب عليه الدية لأن الأصل براءة الذمة وفراغ الساحة، وطريق اشتغالها الشرع، ولم نجد ما يدل على الاشتغال، فبقي على الأصل، فيقول من يعترض عليه : هذا يعارضه أنا أجمعنا على أنه قد اشتغلت ذمته بالقتل، فمن زعم أن بإخراج الكفارة تبرأ ذمته فعليه الدليل والجواب أن يبين المستدل أن القدر الذي دل الدليل على اشتغال الذمة به هو الكفارة، وفيما سوى ذلك فهو باق على البراءة (1) . ومثاله أيضًا : أن يستدل المالكي على أن قاتل العمد لا كفارة عليه، لأن الأصل براءة الذمة وفراغ الساحة فمن ادعى اشتغالها بالكفارة احتاج إلى دليل، فيقول الشافعي أو الحنفي معترضا : هذا يعارضه أنه لما قتله اشتغلت ذمته بالإجماع، فمن زعم أنه إن يغرم القيمة تبرأ ذمته احتاج إلى دليل والجواب أن يبين المستدل أن القدر الذي اتفقا على اشتغال الذمة به هو القيمة خاصة، وفيما سواها ذمته على البراءة، فمن ادعى اشتغالها احتاج إلى دليل وقد أورد الإمام ابن عقيل مجموعة من الأمثلة التي يختلف فيها النظر إلى حكمها الأصلي، فيختلف نتيجة لذلك حكمها العملي عند الوقوع، فقال:
(2)
إننا نجد مسائل معارضة للأصول، فيختلف قول العلماء فيها :
منها : إذا قطع الرجل عضوا باطشًا من رجل واختلفا في سلامته وشلله، فالأصل سلامة العضو، والأصل براءة الجاني مما زاد على صورة العضو من البطش الزائد على غرامة عينه دون بطشه .
فمن الناس من يجعل القول قول الجاني لأن الأصل براءة ذمته، ومنهم من يجعل القول قول المجني عليه لأن الأصل بقاء العضو على سلامته الأصلية وعدم شلله وتعطله .
The third topic: Dialectics. Preliminary: Dialectics in inference by presumption of the state (istishab al-hal). And the meaning of presumption of the state is: 'remaining upon the original ruling'. And the original ruling is permissibility or innocence. So if a legally responsible person is uncertain about the ruling of a matter—whether it is permissible or forbidden—and finds no evidence concerning it from text or analogy, then its ruling is the original one, i.e., permissibility, until evidence concerning it arrives. And the majority of scholars of usul have gone to considering presumption of the state as a legal proof that permits acting upon it in legal rulings. Imam al-Baji said: Know that the ruling of presumption of the state of reason is a valid proof, and this is what the majority of scholars said. And Imam Ibn 'Aqil said: It—i.e., presumption—is a root from the roots of the religion and a proof from the proofs of the law. But the majority of Hanafi scholars and Abu al-Husayn al-Basri and a group of theologians opposed this, and they said that presumption is not considered a legal argument or a legal proof. And the result of this disagreement regarding argumentation by presumption of the state is that inference by it on the part of those who affirm it in practical matters was not free from objections by opponents, and those objections transfer the disagreement from a dispute concerning a practical occurrence to a dialectic about the proof upon which it is based. And presumption of the state is objected to from two aspects: The first demand: Mutual opposition by like. That is, the claimant infers by presumption in the ruling of a matter, then the opponent meets him by objecting to his inference by inferring with a similar presumption on his own view in the same matter. For example, the Shafi'i says concerning one who killed a Muslim in the abode of war while not knowing his Islam: No blood money is due upon him because the original is the innocence of the liability and emptiness of the arena, and the way of its being occupied is the law, and we have not found what indicates occupation, so it remains on the original. Then the one who objects to him says: This is opposed by the fact that we have agreed that his liability has been occupied by the killing. So whoever claims that by bringing forth expiation his liability is cleared, the burden of proof is upon him. And the answer is that the inferrer should clarify that the amount which the evidence indicated the liability is occupied with is the expiation, and regarding other than that, it remains on innocence. Another example: The Maliki infers that the intentional murderer has no expiation, because the original is innocence of liability and emptiness of arena, so whoever claims its occupation by expiation needs a proof. Then the Shafi'i or Hanafi says in objection: This is opposed by the fact that when he killed him, his liability became occupied by consensus, so whoever claims that if he pays the value his liability is cleared needs a proof. And the answer is that the inferrer should clarify that the amount which they agreed upon the liability being occupied with is the value specifically, and regarding other than that, his liability is on innocence, so whoever claims its occupation needs a proof. And Imam Ibn 'Aqil presented a group of examples in which the view of their original ruling differs, and as a result their practical ruling upon occurrence differs. He said: We find matters that oppose the principles, so the scholars' statements differ concerning them. Among them: If a man cuts a limb that has strength from a man, and they differ concerning its soundness and paralysis, the original is the soundness of the limb, and the original is the innocence of the perpetrator from what exceeds the form of the limb in terms of extra strength beyond the fine for its entity without its strength. Some people make the statement that of the perpetrator because the original is the innocence of his liability, and some of them make the statement that of the victim because the original is the limb's remaining on its original soundness and lack of paralysis and disablement.
Source pages p. 480

Commentary
This passage is a dialectical exposition from the field of Usul al-Fiqh, explaining a specific type of objection used in jadal (dialectical argumentation) against the proof known as istishab al-hal (presumption of continuity). The author begins by defining istishab: it means 'remaining upon the original ruling,' where the original ruling is permissibility (ibaha) or innocence (bara'a). When a mukallaf is uncertain about a matter and finds no textual or analogical evidence, the default is permissibility until evidence arises. The author then notes that the majority of usul scholars (like al-Baji and Ibn 'Aqil) consider istishab a valid legal proof, while the Hanafis and Abu al-Husayn al-Basri reject it. This disagreement leads to objections when someone uses istishab in practice; these objections transform a substantive dispute into a dialectical one about the proof itself. The first type of objection is 'mutual opposition by like' (al-mu'arada bi al-mithl): the opponent counters the claimant's istishab with another istishab that supports a different ruling in the same matter. Two examples illustrate this. In the first, a Shafi'i argues that a person who kills a Muslim in dar al-harb unknowingly owes no blood money, based on the original innocence of liability. The opponent objects by saying that the killer's liability was undoubtedly occupied by the act of killing (since killing is a crime), so the burden is on the claimant to prove that expiation clears it. The claimant answers by specifying that the only proven occupation is for expiation, not for blood money. In the second example, a Maliki argues that an intentional murderer owes no kaffarah (expiation) by istishab. The opponent (Shafi'i or Hanafi) objects that the liability was occupied by consensus upon killing, so the claimant must prove that paying the value (diyah) clears it. The answer is to limit the agreed occupation to the value only. Finally, the author quotes Ibn 'Aqil, who gives a third example: if a man cuts another's limb and they dispute whether it was sound or paralyzed before, there are conflicting principles—the original soundness of the limb versus the original innocence of the perpetrator regarding extra damages. Here, the disagreement is about which principle takes precedence.

Key Terms
استصحاب الحال
المعارضة بالمثل
براءة الذمة

Checkpoint
In the example of the intentional murderer, how does the opponent (Shafi'i/Hanafi) object to the Maliki's use of istishab?
A) By denying that istishab is a valid proof altogether.
B) By arguing that the victim's family has a right to blood money.
C) By pointing out that the killer's liability was occupied by consensus upon killing, so the burden is on the claimant to prove expiation clears it.
D) By claiming that the original ruling is prohibition of murder, not permissibility.

Key Takeaway
Mutual opposition forces the claimant to precisely delimit the scope of the established obligation, leaving the rest on the original presumption.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In the example of the person who kills a Muslim unknowingly in dar al-harb, state the opponent's objection as a mu'arada bi al-mithl, then write the claimant's response in the form: 'The agreed-upon occupation is X; for Y, the original innocence remains.'
?

Dialectic -- Jadal
intermediate
8 min
Objecting and Answering with Istishab in Jadal
Author demonstrates the dialectical move of objection by istishab and the required answer.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to construct and respond to an objection using istishab in a jadal exchange, by identifying the agreed-upon liability and delimiting its scope.

Core Idea
Istishab is a double-edged sword: the same principle of original innocence can be used to counter an opponent's claim.

In jadal, debaters often invoke istishab (presumption of continuity of a previous state) to support their position. However, the opposing debater can wield the same tool to object, forcing the original arguer to refine his argument. This lesson unpacks the technique of objecting with istishab and the required answer: clearly delimiting what the agreed-upon liability covers. We will walk through examples from Shafi'i, Maliki, Hanafi, and Hanbali jurisprudence to see the tool in action.

Guided Path
1
Identify the opponent's istishab claim
Find where the opponent uses bara'at al-dhimmah (original innocence of liability) to argue that no obligation exists. For example, a Shafi'i argues no blood money for killing a Muslim unknowingly in dar al-harb because the original is innocence.
2
Locate the agreed-upon occupied liability
Determine what both parties agree is already established as a liability. In the Shafi'i example, both agree that the killer's liability is occupied by the act of killing (or by blood money payment in other examples). This agreed-upon point is the basis for the objection.
3
Formulate the objection using another istishab
The objector says: 'We agree that the liability is occupied (e.g., by the killing). Therefore, whoever claims that paying a certain amount (e.g., expiation) clears it must provide evidence, because the original is that the liability remains occupied until proven otherwise.' This is an istishab against the opponent's istishab.
4
Construct the answer by delimiting the scope
The original arguer must specify exactly what the agreed-upon liability covers. For instance, the Shafi'i answers: 'The evidence only shows that the liability is occupied by expiation (kaffarah); for any other claim, the original innocence remains.' This narrows the dispute.
5
Apply to other examples
Practice with Ibn 'Aqil's examples: the cut limb and the wrapped person. Identify the competing istishabs and see how delimiting the agreed-upon liability resolves the conflict.

بالاعتراض على استدلاله بالاستدلال باستصحاب مماثل على رأيه في ذات المسألة . وذلك مثل أن يقول الشافعي في من قتل مسلمًا في دار الحرب وهو لا يعلم إسلامه : إنه لا يجب عليه الدية لأن الأصل براءة الذمة وفراغ الساحة، وطريق اشتغالها الشرع، ولم نجد ما يدل على الاشتغال، فبقي على الأصل، فيقول من يعترض عليه : هذا يعارضه أنا أجمعنا على أنه قد اشتغلت ذمته بالقتل، فمن زعم أن بإخراج الكفارة تبرأ ذمته فعليه الدليل والجواب أن يبين المستدل أن القدر الذي دل الدليل على اشتغال الذمة به هو الكفارة، وفيما سوى ذلك فهو باق على البراءة (1) . ومثاله أيضًا : أن يستدل المالكي على أن قاتل العمد لا كفارة عليه، لأن الأصل براءة الذمة وفراغ الساحة فمن ادعى اشتغالها بالكفارة احتاج إلى دليل، فيقول الشافعي أو الحنفي معترضا : هذا يعارضه أنه لما قتله اشتغلت ذمته بالإجماع، فمن زعم أنه إن يغرم القيمة تبرأ ذمته احتاج إلى دليل والجواب أن يبين المستدل أن القدر الذي اتفقا على اشتغال الذمة به هو القيمة خاصة، وفيما سواها ذمته على البراءة، فمن ادعى اشتغالها احتاج إلى دليل وقد أورد الإمام ابن عقيل مجموعة من الأمثلة التي يختلف فيها النظر إلى حكمها الأصلي، فيختلف نتيجة لذلك حكمها العملي عند الوقوع، فقال: (2) إننا نجد مسائل معارضة للأصول، فيختلف قول العلماء فيها : منها : إذا قطع الرجل عضوا باطشًا من رجل واختلفا في سلامته وشلله، فالأصل سلامة العضو، والأصل براءة الجاني مما زاد على صورة العضو من البطش الزائد على غرامة عينه دون بطشه . فمن الناس من يجعل القول قول الجاني لأن الأصل براءة ذمته، ومنهم من يجعل القول قول المجني عليه لأن الأصل بقاء العضو على سلامته الأصلية وعدم شلله وتعطله . (1) المعونة في الجدل للشيرازي ص: 269 - 270 . (2) المنهاج في ترتيب الحجاج، للباجي، ص: 219 · ومن ذلك : إذا ضرب ملفوفًا في كساء فقد بنصفه، ثم اختلف الضارب له وأولياء المضروب في حياته، فقال الجاني : كان ميتا، وقال الأولياء : كان حيًا، فإن الأصل بقاء الحياة والأصل براءة الذمة» (1) .
Regarding objecting to his argument by using an argument with an istishab similar to his own view on the same issue. An example: a Shafi'i says about someone who killed a Muslim in dar al-harb while unaware of his Islam: no blood money is obligatory because the original ruling is innocence of the liability and clearness of the repertoire, and the path to liability is the Shari'a, and we found nothing indicating liability, so it remains on the original. The objector says: This is countered by the fact that we all agree that his liability became occupied by the killing, so whoever claims that by paying the expiation his liability becomes cleared must provide evidence. The answer is that the arguer must clarify that the amount that evidence indicated was occupied is the expiation, and regarding other than that, it remains on innocence. Another example: a Maliki argues that the intentional killer has no expiation, because the original ruling is innocence of liability, so whoever claims it is occupied by expiation needs evidence. The Shafi'i or Hanafi objects: This is countered by the fact that when he killed, his liability became occupied by consensus, so whoever claims that by paying the blood money his liability is cleared needs evidence. The answer is that the arguer must clarify that the amount on which they agreed regarding occupation of liability is the blood money specifically, and regarding other than that, his liability is on innocence, so whoever claims its occupation needs evidence. Imam Ibn 'Aqil brought a set of examples where the view differs regarding their original ruling, thus their practical ruling differs upon occurrence. He said: We find issues conflicting with principles, so the scholars' opinions differ therein. Among them: if a man cuts an active limb of another and they differ over its soundness and paralysis, the original ruling is soundness of the limb, and the original ruling is innocence of the perpetrator from what exceeds the form of the limb from extra active power beyond the compensation for the limb itself without its active power. Some people give the word to the perpetrator because the original is innocence of his liability, and some give the word to the victim because the original is continuity of the limb in its original soundness and absence of its paralysis and disability. Among that: if he struck someone wrapped in a garment and broke it in half, then the striker and the victim's guardians differ over his life: the offender said he was dead, and the guardians said he was alive, for the original is continuity of life and the original is innocence of liability.
Source pages p. 481

Commentary
This passage presents the dialectical technique of objecting to an argument by using istishab (presumption of continuity) similar to the opponent's own istishab. The author first explains the move: when a debater uses istishab (e.g., original innocence of liability) to support his claim, the objector can counter by invoking another istishab that applies to the same issue, thereby forcing the original arguer to justify his position against this conflicting presumption. Two concrete examples from Islamic law illustrate this: the case of killing a Muslim unknowingly in dar al-harb (Shafi'i position on blood money) and the case of intentional killing (Maliki position on expiation). In both, the objector uses the consensus that the killer's liability is occupied (by the act of killing or by blood money) to argue that any claim of innocence for other components (expiation or excess) requires evidence. The answer, as the author states, is to delineate precisely what the agreed-upon liability covers and what remains under the original principle. The passage then quotes Ibn 'Aqil, who provides further examples where multiple conflicting istishabs lead to differing rulings, such as the case of a cut limb where original soundness and original innocence conflict, or the case of a wrapped person struck half-dead where original life and original innocence of liability conflict. All examples are drawn from usul al-fiqh and jadal practice, demonstrating how istishab serves as a common ground in dialectical exchanges.

Key Terms
استصحاب
براءة الذمة
القدر الذي اتفقا على اشتغال الذمة به

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
In jadal, istishab can be countered by another istishab; the key to the answer is to delimit the exact scope of the agreed-upon liability.

Dialectic -- Jadal

Transfer of Ruling by Evidence (al-naql bil-dalil) in Jadal
Author defines and exemplifies the dialectical move of transferring a ruling from the original principle to an evidence-based ruling.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and apply the dialectical move of transferring a ruling from istishab to evidence in a jadal debate.

Core Idea
When evidence appears, istishab must yield—unless the evidence is refuted.

In a jadal debate, a claimant often relies on the presumption of continuity (istishab) to support his position. The objector can counter by pointing to a specific evidence that directly addresses the issue, thereby 'transferring' the ruling away from the original presumption. This lesson unpacks that dialectical move and shows how the claimant may defend istishab by rebutting the evidence.

Guided Path
1
Identify the claimant's initial move
The claimant begins by invoking istishab—the presumption of continuity (e.g., original innocence of liability). Note the ruling being presumed.
2
Recognize the objector's response: al-naql bil-dalil
The objector cites a specific evidence (dalil) from Shari'a—textual or ijtihadi—that addresses the same issue, arguing that the ruling must be transferred from the original presumption to this evidence.
The objector's justification: 'there remains no justification for acting on istishab when evidence appears.'
3
Understand the claimant's defense
The claimant can defend istishab by critiquing the evidence—showing it is weak, inapplicable, or outweighed—so that the original presumption remains operative.
This defense is the 'jawab' mentioned in the passage.
4
Apply to an example from Ibn 'Aqil
Consider the wudu examples: the original ruling is 'no wudu' (istishab), but textual evidence (Qur'an/Sunna) or analogy overrides it, transferring the ruling to obligation of wudu. Identify each element: istishab, evidence, transfer.
Ibn 'Aqil's divorce example shows an exception where istishab is not transferred due to practical necessity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المطلب الثاني : النقل بالدليل وذلك أن يستدل المدعي باستصحاب حال البراءة الأصلية، فيواجهه المعترض بأن هناك دليلا في المسألة، فلابد من نقل حكمها إلى ذلك الدليل، إذ لم يبق مبرر للعمل بالاستصحاب، مثاله : أن يأتي المالكي بالدليل السابق ذاته في المسألة ذاتها، فيواجهه المعترض بقوله : إن الأصل براءة الذمة بالعقل، إلا أن الشرع قد دل على اشتغال ذمته . . ثم يذكر ما يستدل به على اشتغال ذمته، أو أن يأتي الشافعي بالدليل السابق ذاته في المسألة ذاتها، فيواجهه المعترض بأن ينقل الحكم عن الأصل بدليل، وهو أن يذكر ما يدل على وجوب الدية، والجواب عنه أن يتكلم المستدل على ما يورده المعترض من الدليل بما يسقط الاستدلال به، حتى تبقى براءة الذمة على موجب العقل ومما هو محل اتفاق بين القائلين بالاستصحاب أن العمل به يترك إذا ورد الدليل بحكم من الشرع في المسألة، سواء كان هذا الدليل لفظا من الشارع أو دليلا اجتهاديًا كالقياس أو غيره . . قال ابن عقيل: اعلم أن هذا الأصل يترك لدليل شرعي بنطق أو استنباط، فالأصل أن لا وضوء من ملامسة امرأة ولا مس ذكر، فأوجبناه بدليل الكتاب والسنة، والأصل أن لا وضوء على من مس الدبر ولا على المرأة إذا مست زوجها، فأوجبناه بالقياس، وإذا قال الرجل لزوجته : إن حضت فأنت طالق ؛ فقالت : قد حضت وكذبها، فهي تدعي الحيض ووقوع الطلاق، والأصل عدمهما، إلا أنه جعل القول قولها لتعذر إقامة البينة وأن الحيض هي أعلم به منه» (3) (1) المعونة في الجدل، للشيرازي ص: 270 ، المنهاج في ترتيب الحجاج، للباجي، ص: 219 . (2) الواضح في أصول الفقه لابن عقيل، ج: 2، ص: 314 . (3) الواضح في أصول الفقه :ج: 2، ص: 314 - 315
The second request: Transfer by evidence. That is, the claimant argues using istishab of the original innocence, then the objector confronts him that there is evidence in the issue, so the ruling must be transferred to that evidence, since there remains no justification for acting on istishab. Example: The Maliki comes with the same previous argument in the same issue, then the objector confronts him by saying: The original is innocence of liability by reason, but the Shari'a has indicated occupation of his liability... then he mentions what is used as evidence for occupation of his liability. Or the Shafi'i comes with the same previous argument in the same issue, then the objector confronts him by transferring the ruling from the original by evidence, i.e., he mentions what indicates the obligation of blood money. The answer is that the arguer speaks about the evidence brought by the objector in a way that nullifies using it as evidence, so that innocence of liability remains on the basis of reason. And it is agreed upon among proponents of istishab that acting upon it is abandoned when evidence of a ruling from the Shari'a appears in the issue, whether this evidence is a textual statement from the Lawgiver or an ijtihadi evidence like analogy or other. Ibn 'Aqil said: Know that this original ruling is abandoned for a shari' evidence by text or derivation. The original is: no wudu from touching a woman or touching the penis; we made it obligatory by evidence of the Book and Sunna. The original is: no wudu for one who touches the anus or for the wife if she touches her husband; we made it obligatory by analogy. And if a man says to his wife: 'If you menstruate, you are divorced', and she says: 'I have menstruated' and he denies her, she claims menstruation and occurrence of divorce, and the original is their absence, except that her word is taken because of the difficulty of establishing evidence and that she knows her menstruation better than him.
Source pages p. 482

Commentary
This passage introduces the second dialectical method: al-naql bil-dalil (transfer of ruling by evidence). The author explains that when a claimant uses istishab (presumption of continuity of the original state), the objector can respond by pointing to a specific evidence (dalil) that addresses the issue, thereby transferring the ruling away from the original presumption. The justification is that once evidence appears, istishab no longer holds. The author gives two parallel examples: a Maliki claimant using istishab (original innocence) about expiation for intentional killing, and a Shafi'i claimant using istishab about blood money; in each case, the objector cites evidence from Shari'a (e.g., a text or analogy) that indicates the liability is occupied differently. The proper answer for the claimant is to critique that evidence to nullify its use, thereby preserving the original presumption. The author then notes the consensus that istishab is abandoned when any shari' evidence—textual or ijtihadi—appears. Ibn 'Aqil provides illustrative examples from wudu obligations and a divorce case: in the wudu examples, the original rule (no wudu) is overridden by textual evidence (Qur'an/Sunna) or by analogy (qiyas). In the divorce example, although the original is absence of menstruation and divorce, the wife's word is taken due to practical necessity, showing an exception to the rule. All examples serve to clarify the dialectical move of transferring the ruling from istishab to evidence.

Key Terms
النقل بالدليل
دليل اجتهادي
استصحاب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

B) The ruling is transferred from istishab to the evidence
C) Both istishab and evidence are considered equally
D) Istishab is strengthened by the evidence

Key Takeaway
Al-naql bil-dalil is a core dialectical move: when evidence appears, the ruling transfers from istishab to the evidence, unless the claimant successfully nullifies the evidence.

Conflicting Presumptions in Istishab: A Jadal Case Study
Fiqh case illustrating conflicting istishab claims.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify conflicting istishab claims in a fiqh dispute and analyze how a mujtahid would weigh them.

Core Idea
When two presumptions clash, the mujtahid must apply rules of preference.

This passage from a jadal manual presents a concrete case where two principles of istishab appear to contradict: the presumption that life continues versus the presumption that a person is free from liability. How does a jurist resolve such a conflict? This lesson walks you through the dialectical reasoning and shows how usul al-fiqh equips the mujtahid to handle such tensions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two conflicting presumptions
Locate the two 'asl' statements in the text: 'al-aṣl baqā' al-ḥayāh' (presumption of continuation of life) and 'al-aṣl barā'at al-dhimmah' (presumption of innocence from liability). These are both instances of istishab.
The first presumption favors the victim's guardians; the second favors the perpetrator.
2
Understand the factual dispute
The striker claims the victim was already dead when struck; the guardians claim he was alive. Neither side has direct evidence, so each relies on a presumption.
The dispute is purely factual – the parties disagree on a past state.
3
Recognise the dialectical tension
Both presumptions are valid in principle, but they point to opposite conclusions. If the victim was alive, the striker is liable (barā'at al-dhimmah is overridden). If he was dead, the striker is not liable (baqā' al-ḥayāh is overridden).
The mujtahid must decide which presumption takes priority.
4
Apply the rules of preference (tarjīḥ)
In usul al-fiqh, when two presumptions conflict, jurists consider factors such as: which presumption establishes a right vs. removes a liability, which is stronger in evidentiary support, or which is more specific. Here, baqā' al-ḥayāh establishes the victim's inviolability, while barā'at al-dhimmah protects the striker. The resolution often depends on additional evidence or a hierarchy of principles.
The text does not give a final ruling; the jadal point is to practice weighing conflicting principles.
5
Articulate the logical structure of the conflict
The dispute can be framed as a jadal exchange: mudda'i (guardians) asserts life; mu'tarid (striker) asserts death. Both rely on istishab. The debate tests the ability to balance competing presumptions.
This case is a classic training exercise in adab al-bahth.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومن ذلك : إذا ضرب ملفوفًا في كساء فقد بنصفه، ثم اختلف الضارب له وأولياء المضروب في حياته، فقال الجاني : كان ميتا، وقال الأولياء : كان حيًا، فإن الأصل بقاء الحياة والأصل براءة الذمة» (1) .
And among that: if he struck [someone] wrapped in a garment and cut him in half, then the striker and the victim's guardians disagreed about his life [at the time of the blow]. The perpetrator said: 'He was dead,' and the guardians said: 'He was alive.' Then the presumption is the continuation of life and the presumption is innocence of liability.
Source pages p. 482

Commentary
This passage provides an example from fiqh used in jadal. It illustrates how istishab (presumption of continuity) is applied when there is disagreement. The case: a person strikes another with a weapon, and the victim is cut in half. The perpetrator claims the victim was already dead, while the guardians claim he was alive. The ruling is based on two presumptions: the presumption that life continues until proven otherwise, and the presumption of original innocence (i.e., that the perpetrator's liability is not established). The example shows how these presumptions interact in a dialectical setting.

Key Takeaway
Conflicting istishab claims require a mujtahid to apply rules of preference based on the type of presumption and the strength of evidence.

The Dialectical Move of Naql bi al-Dalil
Author explains the method of transferring the ruling from istishab to another evidence when a legal proof exists.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the dialectical move of 'naql bi al-dalil' and identify when a claimant's istishab is overturned by an objector's evidence in a jadal exchange.

Core Idea
In jadal, istishab yields to evidence – the objector 'transfers' the ruling to a stronger proof.

This lesson unpacks a key move in dialectical debate (jadal) within usul al-fiqh. The claimant often starts with istishab (presumption of continuity). The objector can counter by pointing to an existing legal evidence. This forces a 'transfer' of the ruling from the original state to that evidence. Understanding this move is crucial for following juristic debates and constructing valid objections.

Guided Path
1
Identify the initial position
The claimant argues using istishab, i.e., presuming the continuity of an original state (e.g., innocence of liability). Recognize the presumption being invoked.
2
Recognize the objector's challenge
The objector asserts that there is a legal evidence (daliil) relevant to the case. This evidence overrides the presumption. Identify the evidence being cited.
3
Understand the 'naql' movement
The objector demands that the ruling be transferred from the original state (istishab) to the evidence. This is the core dialectical move – the 'transfer by evidence' (al-naql bi al-dalil).
4
Analyze the claimant's response
The claimant can respond by disputing the evidence's applicability, authenticity, or strength. If successful, istishab remains. Identify the counter-argument presented.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المطلب الثاني : النقل بالدليل وذلك أن يستدل المدعي باستصحاب حال البراءة الأصلية، فيواجهه المعترض بأن هناك دليلا في المسألة، فلابد من نقل حكمها إلى ذلك الدليل، إذ لم يبق مبرر للعمل بالاستصحاب، مثاله : أن يأتي المالكي بالدليل السابق ذاته في المسألة ذاتها، فيواجهه المعترض بقوله : إن الأصل براءة الذمة بالعقل، إلا أن الشرع قد دل على اشتغال ذمته . . ثم يذكر ما يستدل به على اشتغال ذمته، أو أن يأتي الشافعي بالدليل السابق ذاته في المسألة ذاتها، فيواجهه المعترض بأن ينقل الحكم عن الأصل بدليل، وهو أن يذكر ما يدل على وجوب الدية، والجواب عنه أن يتكلم المستدل على ما يورده المعترض من الدليل بما يسقط الاستدلال به، حتى تبقى براءة الذمة على موجب العقل ومما هو محل اتفاق بين القائلين بالاستصحاب أن العمل به يترك إذا ورد الدليل بحكم من الشرع في المسألة، سواء كان هذا الدليل لفظا من الشارع أو دليلا اجتهاديًا كالقياس أو غيره . . قال ابن عقيل: اعلم أن هذا الأصل يترك لدليل شرعي بنطق أو استنباط، فالأصل أن لا وضوء من ملامسة امرأة ولا مس ذكر، فأوجبناه بدليل الكتاب والسنة، والأصل أن لا وضوء على من مس الدبر ولا على المرأة إذا مست زوجها، فأوجبناه بالقياس، وإذا قال الرجل لزوجته : إن حضت فأنت طالق ؛ فقالت : قد حضت وكذبها، فهي تدعي الحيض ووقوع الطلاق، والأصل عدمهما، إلا أنه جعل القول قولها لتعذر إقامة البينة وأن الحيض هي أعلم به منه» (3) لكن الاتفاق على الأصل لا يعني الاتفاق على الفروع المبنية عليه، فنقض الوضوء بلمس المرأة مثلا ليس محل اتفاق بين القائلين بالاستصحاب أنفسهم، فضلا عن غيرهم.
The second requirement: Transferring by evidence. That is, the claimant argues using istishab of the original state of innocence, and the objector confronts him by saying that there is evidence in the matter, so it is necessary to transfer the ruling to that evidence, since there is no justification for acting on istishab. Example: The Maliki comes with the same previous evidence in the same matter, and the objector confronts him by saying: The original principle is innocence of liability by reason, but the Law has indicated that his liability is established... then he mentions what indicates the establishment of liability. Or the Shafi'i comes with the same previous evidence in the same matter, and the objector confronts him by transferring the ruling from the original principle with evidence, which is to mention what indicates the obligation of blood-money. The response is that the claimant speaks about the evidence presented by the objector in a way that invalidates using it as evidence, so that innocence of liability remains according to reason. And it is agreed upon among proponents of istishab that it is abandoned when a legal ruling comes from the Law in the matter, whether that evidence is a text from the Lawgiver or an ijtihad-based evidence like qiyas or other. Ibn 'Aqil said: Know that this principle is abandoned for a legal evidence by text or derivation. The principle is that there is no wudu' from touching a woman or touching the penis, but we made it obligatory by evidence from Book and Sunna. The principle is that there is no wudu' from touching the anus or for a woman touching her husband, but we made it obligatory by qiyas. If a man says to his wife: 'If you menstruate, then you are divorced,' and she says, 'I have menstruated,' and he denies it, she claims menstruation and occurrence of divorce, and the principle is their non-existence, but her statement is accepted due to the difficulty of establishing proof and because she knows best about her menstruation. However, agreement on the principle does not mean agreement on the branches built upon it. For example, nullification of wudu' by touching a woman is not agreed upon among proponents of istishab themselves, let alone others.
Source pages p. 482

Commentary
This passage is a dialectical exposition on the concept of 'naql bi al-dalil' (transfer by evidence) in jadal. It explains how when a claimant uses istishab (presumption of continuity of the original state), the objector can counter by pointing out that there exists a legal evidence (daliil) in the matter, so the ruling must be transferred to that evidence. The author gives two examples: one regarding a Maliki and one regarding a Shafi'i in a similar case. Then he notes the agreed-upon rule that istishab is abandoned when legal evidence appears. He quotes Ibn 'Aqil, who provides examples from wudu' and divorce. Finally, the author adds a critical observation: agreement on the principle of istishab does not imply agreement on its applications, as e.g., the issue of wudu' by touching a woman is disputed among proponents themselves.

Key Terms
النقل بالدليل
الاستصحاب
المعترض
المدعي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
When does istishab (presumption of continuity) get abandoned according to the passage?
A) When there is a consensus among jurists
B) When a legal evidence (daliil) from the Law appears on the matter
C) When the claimant provides a stronger argument
D) When the objector demands it

Key Takeaway
The dialectical move of al-naql bi al-dalil allows a jurist to overturn a presumption of continuity when a stronger legal evidence is introduced, and the proponent of istishab must then respond to restore it.

Definition and Condition of Mafhum al-Muwafaqa
Defines mafhum al-muwafaqa with two opinions on the priority condition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the two definitions of mafhum al-muwafaqa (agreement only vs. priority condition) and apply the correct condition to a given textual inference.

Core Idea
Mafhum al-muwafaqa extends a ruling to an unmentioned case if it is equally or more deserving.

In this lesson, we explore the foundational concept of mafhum al-muwafaqa, a key tool in usul al-fiqh for deriving rulings from texts. We examine the two main opinions on its definition and learn how to apply the condition of priority, which is central to many juristic arguments.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two definitions
Locate the two opinions: one defines mafhum al-muwafaqa simply as agreement of ruling between mentioned and unmentioned cases; the other adds the condition that the unmentioned case must be more deserving (awla).
The second opinion is stricter and is associated with al-Tilimsani.
2
Extract the condition of 'awla'
Focus on the statement by al-Tilimsani: 'To know that the unmentioned case is more deserving of the ruling than the mentioned one.' This condition requires a stronger rationale for the unmentioned case.
3
Test the condition on an example
Apply the awla condition to the Quranic verse prohibiting saying 'uff' to parents (17:23). Does it imply prohibition of hitting? Hitting is more harmful, so it is more deserving of prohibition, satisfying the awla condition.
This is a clear case of awla, accepted by both definitions.
4
Compare with the equality case
Consider the verse about intercourse until dawn (2:187). The unmentioned case of becoming junub at dawn is equally deserving of the ruling, not more. This example works under the broader definition (agreement) but not under the strict awla condition.
Some scholars accept equality (musawi) as part of mafhum al-muwafaqa.

المسائل (1)
وتارة يكون المفهوم مساويا للمنطوق في استحقاق الحكم، وذلك مثل قوله تعالى : ... فَالْآنَ بَاشِرُوهُنَّ وَابْتَغُوا مَا كَتَبَ اللهُ لَكُمْ وَكُلُوا وَاشْرَبُوا حَتَّى يَتَبَيَّنَ لَكُم الْخَيْطُ الْأَبْيَضُ مِنَ الْخَيْطِ الأَسْوَدِ مِنَ الْفَجْرِ ... (2) ، فقد دل جواز المباشرة حتى الفجر، على جواز أن يصبح الصائم جنبا، لأنه لو لم يجز ذلك لما جاز للصائم مد المباشرة إلى طلوع الفجر، بل كان يجب قطعها بمدار ما يسع فيه الغسل قبل طلوع الفجر ومع أن الاستدلال بمفهوم الموافقة أمر يكاد يكون محل اتفاق، إلا أنه مع ذلك كان اتفاقا في الجملة، أما في التفاصيل فهناك اختلافات فرعية، ولذلك فإن الاستدلال به لا يسلم من الاعتراض.
(3)
فترد عليه ستة أنواع من الاعتراضات :
Prelude: The Fourth Topic: Dialectic regarding inference by the import of discourse (fahwa al-khitab). Fahwa al-khitab is also called 'concept of agreement' (mafhum al-muwafaqa). According to some usulis, it is what indicates that the ruling concerning the unmentioned case agrees with the ruling concerning the mentioned case. Some other scholars of usul al-fiqh held that among the conditions for ruling by the concept of agreement is that the unmentioned case be more deserving of the ruling than the mentioned one. For example, al-Tilimsani defined it as: 'To know that the unmentioned case is more deserving of the ruling than the mentioned one.' So the concept is sometimes more deserving of the ruling than the mentioned case, like the indication of the prohibition of saying 'uff' [Quran 17:23] for the prohibition of hitting and all types of harm, which is more severe than saying 'uff'. That is like His saying: 'So whoever does an atom's weight of good will see it, and whoever does an atom's weight of evil will see it' [Quran 99:7-8]. If an atom's weight of action is rewarded if good and punished if evil, then what is greater than that is more deserving of reward or punishment. This type is either clear (jali) or obscure (khafi). The clear is definitive because the suitability of the ruling to the derivative case appears very clearly, as in the previous examples. The obscure is speculative; even though the derivative case appears more deserving of the ruling than the original, it remains probable and subject to inference (ijtihad), as in the Maliki position that a deliberate abandoner of prayer is obligated to make it up, based on the hadith: 'Whoever sleeps through a prayer or forgets it, let him pray it when he remembers it.' They said: If the sleeping and forgetful person makes up the prayer even though they are not addressed [with the immediate obligation], then making it up by the deliberate abandoner is more deserving. This is because the objector can deny the priority by saying: It does not follow from the obligation of making up the prayer for the sleeper and forgetful one that it is obligatory for the deliberate abandoner, because making up is a compensation; perhaps the sin of deliberately abandoning it is too great to be compensated. For this reason, disagreement abounds in this type of issues. Sometimes the concept is equal to the mentioned case in entitlement to the ruling, like His saying: '... so now have sexual relations with them and seek what Allah has decreed for you, and eat and drink until the white thread of dawn becomes distinct from the black thread' [Quran 2:187]. The permissibility of intercourse until dawn indicates the permissibility of a fasting person becoming junub at dawn, because if that were not permissible, then it would not be permissible for the fasting person to extend intercourse until dawn; rather, they would have to stop it at a time that allows for ghusl before dawn. Although inference by the concept of agreement is almost a point of consensus, still it is consensus in general; regarding details, there are subsidiary differences, and therefore inference by it does not escape objection. Six types of objections are raised against it:
Source pages p. 484

Commentary
This passage defines the concept of agreement (mafhum al-muwafaqa), a key tool in legal dialectic. It begins by noting two opinions among usulis: one defines it as where the ruling of the unmentioned case agrees with the mentioned case; the other adds the condition that the unmentioned case must be more deserving (awla) of the ruling. The author then presents examples: the Quranic prohibition of saying 'uff' to parents implies prohibition of hitting them, which is more harmful—thus the unmentioned case is more deserving. Similarly, the atom's weight verses show that a greater good or evil is more deserving of reward or punishment. The passage distinguishes between a clear (jali) type, where the suitability is obvious and the inference is certain, and an obscure (khafi) type, which remains probable and open to ijtihad. The Maliki example of deliberately missed prayers illustrates the latter: although making up prayer for the forgetful seems to make it even more obligatory for the deliberate abandoner, the objector can block the priority by arguing that the deliberate sin may be too severe for compensation. Finally, the passage gives an example of equal entitlement: the permission of intercourse until dawn implies that a fasting person may become junub at dawn, as the ruling is equally applicable. The passage concludes that despite general consensus on the principle, detailed disagreements remain, and six types of objections can be raised against such inference.

According to the stricter definition of mafhum al-muwafaqa (al-Tilimsani's view), what additional condition must be met for the inference to be valid?
A) The unmentioned case must be exactly equal in ruling.
B) The unmentioned case must be more deserving of the ruling than the mentioned case.
C) The unmentioned case must be mentioned explicitly in another verse.
D) The ruling must be based on a clear analogy (qiyas jali).

Key Takeaway
Mafhum al-muwafaqa extends a ruling to an unmentioned case when the unmentioned case is equally or more deserving; the stricter view requires priority (awla).

Types of Mafhum al-Muwafaqa: Awla and Musawi
Explains awla and musawi types with Quranic examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify whether a given inference is based on priority (awla) or equality (musawi) and provide examples from Islamic texts.

Core Idea
Mafhum al-muwafaqa works either when the unmentioned case is more deserving or equally deserving of the ruling.

In this lesson, we delve into the two categories of mafhum al-muwafaqa: awla (where the unmentioned case has a stronger rationale) and musawi (where it shares the same rationale). We use Quranic examples to illustrate each, including the famous case of the atom's weight verses and the permissibility of intercourse until dawn.

Guided Path
1
Identify the type from the rationale
Determine whether the unmentioned case has a stronger reason for the ruling than the mentioned case (awla) or an equally strong reason (musawi).
2
Analyze the 'uff' example as awla
The prohibition of saying 'uff' implies prohibition of hitting because hitting is more harmful. The rationale (harm to parents) is stronger for hitting, so it is awla.
This is a clear case of priority.
3
Analyze the atom's weight example as awla
If an atom's weight of good is rewarded, then a larger good is more deserving of reward. This is another awla example from Quran 99:7-8.
4
Analyze the intercourse example as musawi
The permissibility of intercourse until dawn (Quran 2:187) equally implies the permissibility of becoming junub at dawn. The rationale is the same: continuity until fajr does not invalidate the fast.
Here the unmentioned case is equal in entitlement, not stronger.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Using the Quranic verse 'And do not kill your children for fear of poverty' (17:31), determine whether the prohibition of killing adults for fear of poverty is awla or musawi. Explain your reasoning.
?

Clear and Obscure Inferences in Mafhum al-Muwafaqa
Distinguishes jali and khafi subtypes with the Maliki example of deliberate prayer abandonment.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to differentiate between clear (jali) and obscure (khafi) types of mafhum al-muwafaqa based on certainty level and provide examples from Islamic legal debates.

Core Idea
Jali inferences are certain; khafi inferences are probable and subject to ijtihad.

This lesson examines the subdivision of mafhum al-muwafaqa into clear (jali) and obscure (khafi) types. We see how the Maliki example of deliberate prayer abandonment leads to disagreement because the inference is khafi, while examples like the 'uff' verse are jali and certain.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two subtypes: jali and khafi
Locate the definition: jali (clear) is definitive because the suitability is obvious; khafi (obscure) is probable and open to ijtihad.
2
Recognize jali in the 'uff' example
The prohibition of hitting from the 'uff' verse is clear because causing harm is obviously more severe. No rational doubt exists, so it is jali.
The suitability of the ruling is self-evident.
3
Analyze khafi in the Maliki example
The Maliki inference that deliberate abandoners of prayer must make it up is khafi because the objector can block the analogy: the sin of deliberate abandonment may be too great for compensation. Thus the inference is probable, not certain.
The objector denies the priority (awla) by challenging the rationale.
4
Assess the impact of objections
Khafi inferences are subject to debate and are common in disputed issues (masa'il khilafiyya). The passage notes that many disagreements arise from such cases.

فترد عليه ستة أنواع من الاعتراضات :
Prelude: The Fourth Topic: Dialectic regarding inference by the import of discourse (fahwa al-khitab). Fahwa al-khitab is also called 'concept of agreement' (mafhum al-muwafaqa). According to some usulis, it is what indicates that the ruling concerning the unmentioned case agrees with the ruling concerning the mentioned case. Some other scholars of usul al-fiqh held that among the conditions for ruling by the concept of agreement is that the unmentioned case be more deserving of the ruling than the mentioned one. For example, al-Tilimsani defined it as: 'To know that the unmentioned case is more deserving of the ruling than the mentioned one.' So the concept is sometimes more deserving of the ruling than the mentioned case, like the indication of the prohibition of saying 'uff' [Quran 17:23] for the prohibition of hitting and all types of harm, which is more severe than saying 'uff'. That is like His saying: 'So whoever does an atom's weight of good will see it, and whoever does an atom's weight of evil will see it' [Quran 99:7-8]. If an atom's weight of action is rewarded if good and punished if evil, then what is greater than that is more deserving of reward or punishment. This type is either clear (jali) or obscure (khafi). The clear is definitive because the suitability of the ruling to the derivative case appears very clearly, as in the previous examples. The obscure is speculative; even though the derivative case appears more deserving of the ruling than the original, it remains probable and subject to inference (ijtihad), as in the Maliki position that a deliberate abandoner of prayer is obligated to make it up, based on the hadith: 'Whoever sleeps through a prayer or forgets it, let him pray it when he remembers it.' They said: If the sleeping and forgetful person makes up the prayer even though they are not addressed [with the immediate obligation], then making it up by the deliberate abandoner is more deserving. This is because the objector can deny the priority by saying: It does not follow from the obligation of making up the prayer for the sleeper and forgetful one that it is obligatory for the deliberate abandoner, because making up is a compensation; perhaps the sin of deliberately abandoning it is too great to be compensated. For this reason, disagreement abounds in this type of issues. Sometimes the concept is equal to the mentioned case in entitlement to the ruling, like His saying: '... so now have sexual relations with them and seek what Allah has decreed for you, and eat and drink until the white thread of dawn becomes distinct from the black thread' [Quran 2:187]. The permissibility of intercourse until dawn indicates the permissibility of a fasting person becoming junub at dawn, because if that were not permissible, then it would not be permissible for the fasting person to extend intercourse until dawn; rather, they would have to stop it at a time that allows for ghusl before dawn. Although inference by the concept of agreement is almost a point of consensus, still it is consensus in general; regarding details, there are subsidiary differences, and therefore inference by it does not escape objection. Six types of objections are raised against it:
Source pages p. 484

Commentary
This passage defines the concept of agreement (mafhum al-muwafaqa), a key tool in legal dialectic. It begins by noting two opinions among usulis: one defines it as where the ruling of the unmentioned case agrees with the mentioned case; the other adds the condition that the unmentioned case must be more deserving (awla) of the ruling. The author then presents examples: the Quranic prohibition of saying 'uff' to parents implies prohibition of hitting them, which is more harmful—thus the unmentioned case is more deserving. Similarly, the atom's weight verses show that a greater good or evil is more deserving of reward or punishment. The passage distinguishes between a clear (jali) type, where the suitability is obvious and the inference is certain, and an obscure (khafi) type, which remains probable and open to ijtihad. The Maliki example of deliberately missed prayers illustrates the latter: although making up prayer for the forgetful seems to make it even more obligatory for the deliberate abandoner, the objector can block the priority by arguing that the deliberate sin may be too severe for compensation. Finally, the passage gives an example of equal entitlement: the permission of intercourse until dawn implies that a fasting person may become junub at dawn, as the ruling is equally applicable. The passage concludes that despite general consensus on the principle, detailed disagreements remain, and six types of objections can be raised against such inference.

خفي

Key Takeaway
Clear (jali) mafhum al-muwafaqa is certain; obscure (khafi) is probable and often leads to disagreement because it can be challenged by valid objections.

The Objection of Demanding Verification of the Cause
First objection to mafhum al-muwafaqa: demanding proof of the cause.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and analyze the objection of demanding verification of the cause (i'tirad bi-al-mutalaba) in a jadal exchange and evaluate its impact on analogical reasoning in usul al-fiqh.

Core Idea
Challenge the cause, not the ruling: the objector attacks the very basis of the analogy.

In jadal (dialectical debate), a common tactic is to challenge the opponent's claimed cause ('illa) before it can be used to extend a ruling. This lesson introduces the first of six classical objections to mafhum al-muwafaqa: the demand for verification of the cause. We will walk through the structure of this objection using a classic fiqh debate between a Shafi'i and a Hanafi over expiation for murder.

Guided Path
1
Identify the proponent's claim and inferred conclusion
Locate what the proponent (Shafi'i) asserts as the cause ('illa) for the ruling in the original case, and how he extends it to the branch case via mafhum al-muwafaqa. Here, the cause is 'removing sin' for expiation in intentional murder, extended to unintentional murder a fortiori.
The inference is a fortiori (awlawi), meaning 'if it applies in a lesser case, it applies more in the greater case.'
2
Express the objector's challenge
The objector (Hanafi) does not deny the ruling itself but demands proof that the claimed cause is correct. He points out an inconsistency: if expiation were for sin removal, it wouldn't apply to unintentional killing (where there is no sin). Thus the cause fails.
This is the core of 'demanding verification of the meaning' (tashih al-ma'na). The objector says: 'Prove that your cause is the real cause.'
3
Examine the proponent's response
The Shafi'i responds by reaffirming that expiation is 'made for sin' (al-kaffara ju'ilat li-al-ithm). The passage ends here, but in a full debate he would need to provide evidence (e.g., Quran, hadith) that the purpose of expiation is indeed to cover sin.
The response must defend the cause by showing it is consistent across cases or by redefining the cause more precisely.
4
Assess the dialectical outcome
At this stage, the objector has successfully blocked the analogy by casting doubt on the cause. The proponent must now either provide proof or refine his understanding of the cause. This shows how the objection functions to force deeper investigation.
In jadal rules, the objector wins the round if the proponent cannot defend the cause.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المطلب الأول : الاعتراض بالمطالبة بتصحيح المعنى الذي يقتضي تأكيد الفرع على الأصل وذلك أن يستدل المدعي بمفهوم الموافقة باعتبار أن علة الحكم في الأصل موجودة بشكل أولوي أو موافق فيعترض عليه المخالف بمطالبته بإثبات أن علة الحكم هي ما ادعاه ويطالبه بإثباتها بالدليل، ومثاله : أن يقول الشافعي في إيجاب الكفارة في قتل العمد : إنما وجبت الكفارة لتغطية المأثم أو رفعه، فإذا وجبت في قتل الخطأ ولا إثم فيه، ففي العمد أولى، فيقول الحنفي أو الحنبلي: لا أسلم أن الكفارة وضعت لرفع المأثم، لأنها لو كانت لرفع المأثم لما وجبت في قتل الخطأ لأنه لا مأثم فيه والجواب أن يقول الشافعي : الكفارة جعلت للإثم،
The First Requirement: The objection by demanding verification of the meaning (cause) that requires affirming the derivative case over the original. That is, when the claimant infers using the concept of agreement on the basis that the cause of the ruling in the original exists in a priority or equivalent manner, the opponent objects by demanding proof that the cause of the ruling is what he claimed, and demands its establishment by evidence. An example: The Shafi'i says concerning the obligation of expiation (kaffara) for intentional murder: 'The expiation was imposed to cover or remove the sin. Since it is obligatory in unintentional killing where there is no sin, then it is more deserving in intentional killing.' The Hanafi or Hanbali says: 'I do not concede that expiation was established to remove the sin, because if it were to remove the sin, it would not have been obligatory in unintentional killing, since there is no sin in it.' The answer is that the Shafi'i says: 'Expiation was made for sin.'
Source pages p. 485

Commentary
This passage details the first of six objections that can be raised against an inference by mafhum al-muwafaqa, specifically the objection called 'demand for verification of the meaning' (al-mutalaba bi-tashih al-ma'na). The objection challenges the proponent's claim that the effective cause ('illa) in the original case also applies to the derivative case. The objector demands proof that the asserted cause is indeed the correct one and that it exists in the derivative case. The example given is from fiqh: Shafi'i argues that expiation (kaffara) is required in intentional murder because it expiates sin; since it is required in unintentional murder (where there is no sin), then it is a fortiori required in intentional murder. The Hanafi/Hanbali objector denies that the cause of expiation is sin removal, pointing out that if it were, it would not apply to unintentional killing where there is no sin. Thus, the objector forces the Shafi'i to prove that the cause is indeed sin. The response of the Shafi'i is to assert that expiation is 'made for sin,' but the passage ends before completing the response, indicating that further justification is needed. This exchange illustrates a standard dialectical move in usul: the objector targets the cause itself to block the analogy.

Key Terms
اعتراض بالمطالبة
تصحيح المعنى
علة الحكم
كفارة

Key Takeaway

Annexing a Rare Case to the Predominant in Juristic Analogy
Author argues for ilhaq bil-ghalib in extending kaffara.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply ilhaq bil-ghalib to extend a ruling from a predominant case to a rare case in a juristic analogy.

Core Idea
Ilhaq bil-ghalib treats a rare case as if it were predominant, because the law looks to the majority.

When a rare case shares the same underlying cause as the predominant, jurists may annex it to the predominant for the ruling. This passage from a dialectical argument shows how kaffara for killing is extended to unintentional killing even when negligence is absent, because the predominant case involves negligence. You will learn to identify and construct such analogies.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Original Ruling
Find the crimes for which kaffara is obligatory: killing, breaking fast, violating ihram, killing game, prohibited acts in Hajj, and false oaths. The ruling is that kaffara must be paid.
The term 'kaffara' itself indicates expiation.
2
Pinpoint the Rare Case
The rare case is unintentional killing without any negligence or lack of caution. This is said to be rare (nadir) within the genus of killing.
The author asserts that such an instance is negligible in frequency.
3
Determine the Predominant Attribute
The predominant attribute (ghalib) of unintentional killing is that it involves some degree of negligence or failure to take precautions. The law considers this the typical scenario.
This attribute is what makes it similar to other crimes that require kaffara.
4
Assess Negligibility
The rare case (no negligence) is so infrequent that it can be disregarded for legal purposes. The ruling for the predominant is extended to cover it.
The author supports with parallels: a traveler with an easy journey is still given concessions like the majority of travelers, and a menopausal woman or minor girl observes waiting period like those who might be pregnant.
5
Construct the Analogy
Formulate the analogy: The set of crimes requiring kaffara (e.g., breaking fast, killing game) all involve violation (hathk). Unintentional killing is also a violation, and predominantly involves negligence, so it is annexed. Even the rare case without negligence is included by ilhaq bil-ghalib.
This is an analogical extension, not a deductive proof.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والدلالة على أنها وضعت لذلك؛ تسميتها بكفارة وكذلك قوله عليه الصلاة والسلام في عتق الرقبة في قتل الخطأ : اعتقوا عنه رقبة، يعتق الله بكل عضو منها عضوا منه من النار. وإذا لحظ وضعها وأنها متتبع بإيجابها مواضع الجرائم والهتوك، فلا تتعدى قتلا، أو هتك صيام أو إحرام أو قتل صيد أو ارتكاب محظور في الحج، أو هتك حرمة اسم أقسم به علم بذلك أنها وضعت مكفرة للذنوب، وشذ قتل من بين هذه الجرائم فوجبت فيه، لأنه صورة جريمة، فهو قتل نفس بغير حق، وغالب الحال فيه أنه لا يخلو من تفريط في الاحتياط والتأمل، وإن لم يكن فهو نادر من الجنس، فألحق بالغالب كما ألحق المتودع في سفره في استباحة الرخص بالغالب من المسافرين من أرباب المشاق وكالآيسة في العدة والطفلة ألحقا بمن يتصور في حقهن شغل الرحم بإيجاب العدة والاستبراء
And the indication that it was placed for that is: its name 'kaffara', and likewise his saying, peace be upon him, regarding freeing a slave for unintentional killing: 'Free a slave for him, Allah will free from the Fire a member for each member.' And when one considers its placement and that it follows by its obligation the places of crimes and violations, it does not go beyond killing, or violation of fasting, or ihram, or killing game, or committing a prohibited act in Hajj, or violating the sanctity of a name by which one swore. It is known by that that it was placed as an expiation for sins. And killing stood out among these crimes, so it was obligated therein, because it is a form of crime, it is killing a soul without right, and the prevailing case is that it is not free from negligence in caution and consideration. And if it is not, then it is rare in the genus, so it was annexed to the prevailing, just as the traveler entrusted with his travel for the permissibility of concessions was annexed to the prevailing among travelers who have hardships, and just as the menopausal woman and the minor girl in the waiting period were annexed to those for whom occupation of the womb can be conceived by requiring the waiting period and istibra.
Source pages p. 486

Commentary
This passage is a dialectical argument for the extension of kaffara (expiation) to unintentional killing by predominant analogy (ilhaq bil-ghalib). The author first notes that the term 'kaffara' itself and the hadith about freeing a slave indicate that kaffara is meant for expiating sins. He observes that kaffara is imposed in specific places of crimes: killing, breaking fast, violating ihram, killing game, committing prohibited acts in Hajj, and swearing falsely. Among these, killing stands out as a crime involving killing a soul without right. The prevailing case (ghalib) is that such killing involves negligence, so it is like the others. Even if there is no negligence (rare), it is annexed to the predominant. The author supports this analogy with parallels: a traveler on a short journey is treated like the majority of travelers for concessions, and a menopausal woman or minor girl is required to observe waiting period like those who might be pregnant, due to predominant cases. Thus, unintentional killing is annexed to the crimes requiring kaffara by ilhaq bil-ghalib.

إلحاق بالغالب
الكفارة

Key Takeaway
Ilhaq bil-ghalib allows jurists to extend rulings to rare cases that are negligible in frequency, by treating them as if they shared the predominant attribute.
