The Four Types of Questions in Jadal
Author defines question and answer and lists four types.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the four types of questions in jadal and explain the roles of mustadill and mu'tarid in the question-answer structure.

Core Idea
Jadal is a structured exchange of questions and answers between arguer and objector.

This passage lays out the backbone of all jadal: the alternating question and answer between the one who argues (mustadill) and the one who objects (mu'tarid). Understanding the definitions of 'question' and 'answer' from Ibn 'Aqil and al-Juwayni, and the four types of questions, gives you the map to follow any dialectical exchange in usul al-fiqh. The key is to see that each question targets a specific layer of the argument.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two roles
In jadal, there are always two parties: al-mustadill (the one who presents and defends a position) and al-mu'tarid (the one who raises questions and objections). Locate these terms in the passage and note their functions.
2
Understand the definitions of question and answer
Ibn 'Aqil defines the question as an attempt to transfer the opponent from his madhhab by disputation. Al-Juwayni defines the answer as a report that contains the meaning of the question. These definitions emphasize that the question has a strategic purpose and the answer must be responsive.
3
List the four types of questions
The passage enumerates them: (1) question about the madhhab (the position itself), (2) question about the dalil (the evidence), (3) question about the wajh dalāla (the implication of the evidence), and (4) question by way of i'tirād wa qadḥ (direct objection). Each type zooms in deeper on the argument.
The fourth type is further divided into seven forms, which will be covered in the next lesson.
4
Distinguish the types by their target
Test each type: if the mu'tarid asks 'What is your position?', that is type 1. If he asks 'What is your proof?', that is type 2. If he asks 'How does that proof support your position?', that is type 3. If he directly attacks the proof (e.g., 'That proof is invalid'), that is type 4.
5
Apply the taxonomy to a real debate
Consider a debate on whether qiyas (analogical reasoning) is a valid source of law. The mu'tarid can ask: 'What is your position on qiyas?' (type 1), 'What evidence do you have from the Quran?' (type 2), 'How does that verse imply the validity of qiyas?' (type 3), or 'Your analogy fails because the 'illa is not effective' (type 4). Identify which type is being used.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
16 - يقوم الجدل في كل مراحله وفي كلتا طريقتي المتقدمين والمتأخرين على السؤال والجواب المتبادلين بين المستدل والمعترض والسؤال في الجدل كما عرفه ابن عقيل هو الذي يقصد به نقل الخصم عن مذهبه بطريق المحاجة»، أما الجواب فهو كما عرفه الجويني الخبر المضمن بمعنى السؤال .. والسؤال والجواب في الجدل الأصولي أربعة أنواع : السؤال عن المذهب والجواب عنه، السؤال عن الدليل والجواب عنه السؤال عن وجه دلالة الدليل والجواب عنه، السؤال على وجه الاعتراض والقدح والجواب عنه، وهذا النوع الأخير يرد بصور متعددة، منها: الاستفسار المنع النقض المعارضة القلب، الإلزام، القول بالموجب.
Jadal in all its stages and in both methods of the early and later scholars is based on the mutual question and answer between the arguer (mustadill) and the objector (mu'tarid). The question in jadal, as defined by Ibn 'Aqil, is 'that by which it is intended to transfer the opponent from his school of thought by way of disputation (muhājja).' As for the answer, it is, as defined by al-Juwayni, 'the report that contains the meaning of the question.' And the question and answer in usuli jadal are of four types: (1) question about the school (madhhab) and answer about it; (2) question about the evidence (dalīl) and answer about it; (3) question about the aspect of implication of the evidence (wajh dalāla al-dalīl) and answer about it; (4) question by way of objection and disparagement (i'tirād wa qadḥ) and answer about it. This last type appears in multiple forms including: seeking clarification (istifhām), prevention (man'), invalidation (naqḍ), contradiction (mu'āraḍa), inversion (qalb), obligation (ilzām), and speaking according to what is entailed (al-qawl bi al-mūjib).
Source pages p. 498

Commentary
This passage explains the fundamental structure of jadal as alternating question and answer between the one presenting the case (mustadill) and the objector (mu'tarid). It quotes definitions from Ibn 'Aqil (for question) and al-Juwayni (for answer). Then it classifies questions and answers into four types based on focus: the school of thought, the evidence, the implication of the evidence, and direct objection. The fourth type is further subdivided into seven forms: istifhām, man', naqḍ, mu'āraḍa, qalb, ilzām, and al-qawl bi al-mūjib. This is a detailed taxonomy of dialectical moves.

Key Terms
المستدل
المعترض
السؤال عن وجه دلالة الدليل
المنع
النقض

D) Question by way of objection (i'tirād wa qadḥ)

Key Takeaway
The four types of questions in jadal correspond to the layers of an argument: the position, the evidence, the logical link, and direct attack.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

The Seven Forms of Objection in Jadal
Author lists seven subtypes of the objection question.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish among the seven forms of objection in jadal: istifham, man', naqd, mu'arada, qalb, ilzam, and al-qawl bi al-mujib, and apply them to usuli debates.

Core Idea
The mu'tarid has a precise toolkit of seven objection forms to challenge any argument.

The fourth type of question in jadal – the objection – is the most dynamic, with seven distinct forms. Each form attacks the argument from a different angle: seeking clarification, denying a premise, providing a counterexample, offering a contradictory case, inverting the reasoning, showing an unavoidable consequence, or accepting the premise but denying the conclusion. Mastering these forms allows you to construct powerful objections and recognize them when they are used against you.

Guided Path
1
List the seven forms from the passage
The passage gives them in rapid succession: istifham (seeking clarification), man' (prevention/denial of a premise), naqd (invalidation by counterexample), mu'arada (contradiction by offering a conflicting case), qalb (inversion of the argument), ilzam (obligation/forcing a contradiction), and al-qawl bi al-mujib (accepting the premise but denying the conclusion). Write them down in order.
2
Define each form with a usuli example
For each form, construct a concrete example from usul al-fiqh. Example: Man' – 'I deny that this hadith is authentic.' Naqd – 'You claim intoxicating is the 'illa for prohibition, but I find intoxicating substances that are not prohibited.' Mu'arada – 'You base your ruling on verse X, but verse Y contradicts that ruling.' This step grounds the abstract list in real arguments.
3
Distinguish between closely related forms
Man' and naqd are often confused. Man' is a direct denial of a premise ('Your premise is false'), while naqd offers a specific counterexample that shows the premise fails to lead to the conclusion. Similarly, qalb reverses the roles of 'illa and hukm, while ilzam forces the opponent into a logical trap. The passage distinguishes them by their function.
4
Identify the form in a given objection
Given a sample objection from a debate, decide which of the seven forms it represents. For instance: 'You claim that wine is forbidden because it is intoxicating. But I say that your reasoning, if applied to dates, would make dates forbidden too – which you do not accept.' This is qalb (inversion) because the mustadill's own logic is used to derive an absurd result.
5
Practice constructing each form
Take a simple argument: 'The Quran says 'do not approach prayer while intoxicated' (4:43), therefore alcohol is forbidden.' Produce one version of each objection form against this argument. This exercise will internalize the taxonomy.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Inqita' in Jadal: Definition and Initial Types
Author defines inqita' and begins to list its types (text cuts off).

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the two main types of inqita' mentioned by the author and explain al-Baji's definition.

Core Idea
Inqita' is the stalemate that ends a dialectical exchange when one party cannot continue.

Jadal has a clear end: when a disputant can no longer defend their position. This lesson introduces the concept of inqita' (interruption/stalemate) as defined by al-Baji and begins to categorize its forms. Understanding these types is crucial for analysing legal and theological debates.

Guided Path
1
Identify the key term
Find the word 'الانقطاع' and note that it refers to the inability to continue in a debate. This is the opposite of being able to sustain one's argument.
The term is derived from the root q-t-', implying cutting off.
2
Extract al-Baji's definition
Locate the definition: 'عجز أحد المتناظرين عن تصحيح قوله'. This defines inqita' as the inability of one disputant to correct their statement. Correcting here means providing proof or rebuttal.
3
Distinguish the two mentioned types
The author says the interruption can be from the mustadill (the respondent defending a claim) or from the sa'il (the questioner/objector). This is a basic distinction: either the defender fails to answer objections, or the objector fails to bring a valid objection.
A third type is implied but cut off; think about what it might be (e.g., simultaneous interruption or a draw).
4
Reflect on the practical scenario
Imagine a debate between a Hanafi and a Shafi'i on the validity of qiyas. If the Hanafi cannot refute the Shafi'i's objection, that is inqita' from the mustadill. If the Shafi'i cannot produce a valid objection to the Hanafi's proof, that is inqita' from the sa'il.
This shows the real-world application in usul al-fiqh debates.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Al-Bājī defined it as 'the inability of one of the two disputants to correct his statement...' Two Methods of Jadal in Usul al-Fiqh
Author describes the two historical methods of jadal: the method of the early scholars and the method of the later scholars.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the method of the early scholars (tariqat al-mutaqaddimin) and the method of the later scholars (tariqat al-muta'akhkhirin) in dialectical writing.

Core Idea
Early scholars pair each objection with its response; later scholars group all objections first, then all responses.

This passage explains two organizational styles for dialectical (jadal) discussions in usul al-fiqh. When you open a classical usul work, the way arguments and counterarguments are arranged can tell you which scholarly era it belongs to. Understanding these two methods will help you navigate the text more efficiently.

Guided Path
1
Identify the basic feature of jadal methodology
The author states that usul scholars cover all disputed issues using the dialectical method. Read the passage to understand that jadal involves presenting proofs or objections from opponents and then responding to them.
2
Distinguish the two methods by their structure
The early scholars' method presents each proof/objection together with its response immediately. The later scholars' method presents all proofs/objections together first, then all responses together. Look for whether the response follows immediately after each objection or is delayed.
3
Compare the advantages mentioned
The author notes the early method is easier to understand (aysar lil-fahm) because each argument is immediately refuted. The later method is more concise (akthar ikhtisaran) but not as easy. This trade-off is a clue to which method a text uses.
4
Practice identifying the method from a real title
Try to recall or look up a known usul work: Al-Mu'tamad by Abu al-Husayn al-Basri uses the early method, while Al-Mustasfa by al-Ghazali uses the later method. Knowing this helps you predict the structure before reading.

15 - ذلك عن منهج الأصوليين في الجدل، أما طريقتهم العملية، فتجرى على عدد من المراحل، فهذه الطريقة لها ميزة أساس أو طابع عام، وهي الأصوليين يستوفون عادة كافة المسائل الخلافية في أصول الفقه بالطريقة الجدلية، وهي في الحقيقة طريقتان : طريقة المتقدمين القائمة على إيراد أدلة أو اعتراضات المخالفين وكل دليل أو اعتراض مقرون بما يرد عليه أو يجيب عنه، وميزة هذه الطريقة أنها أيسر للفهم.. وطريقة المتأخرين التي تقوم على إيراد الأدلة أو الاعتراضات مجتمعة متتابعة بعضها إثر ،بعض ثم إيراد الأجوبة التي ترد عليها مجموعة بعضها إثر ،بعض وميزة هذه الطريقة أنها أكثر اختصارًا إلا أنها ليست ميسورة مثل الأولى.
15 - That concerning the methodology of the usul scholars in jadal. As for their practical method, it proceeds through a number of stages. This method has a basic feature or general character: the usul scholars usually cover all disputed issues in usul al-fiqh by the dialectical method. It is in fact two methods: the method of the early scholars, based on presenting the proofs or objections of the opponents, each proof or objection accompanied by what refutes it or responds to it; the advantage of this method is that it is easier to understand. And the method of the later scholars, which is based on presenting the proofs or objections gathered consecutively, one after the other, then presenting the responses that refute them, gathered consecutively, one after the other. The advantage of this method is that it is more concise, but it is not as easy as the first.
Source pages p. 498

Commentary
This passage describes the methodology of usul scholars in jadal (dialectical debate). The author states that usul scholars typically cover all disputed issues in usul al-fiqh using the dialectical method. He then distinguishes two practical methods: the method of the early scholars (mutaqaddimin) and the method of the later scholars (muta'akhkhirin). The early method presents each proof or objection of the opponent together with its refutation or response immediately. The author notes that this method is easier to understand because each argument is followed by its response. The later method groups all proofs or objections together first, and then gives all responses consecutively. This method is more concise but not as easy as the first. This distinction is important for understanding the structure of classical usul works. For example, the early method is seen in works like al-Mu'tamad by Abu al-Husayn al-Basri, while the later method is more common in later texts like al-Mustasfa by al-Ghazali.

The Four Types of Question-Answer Pairs in Jadal
Author defines su'al and jawab and enumerates four types of question-answer pairs in jadal.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the four types of question-answer pairs in jadal and distinguish the seven forms of objection within the fourth type.

Core Idea
Jadal is driven by structured question-answer pairs; mastering them is key to debate.

This lesson breaks down the core exchange of jadal: the question and answer. The author, building on definitions from Ibn Aqil and al-Juwayni, classifies all dialectical interactions into four main types. Understanding this taxonomy will allow you to parse any jadal exchange and see how objections like man' and naqd function within the system.

Guided Path
1
Identify the participants
Note that jadal always involves a proponent (mustadill) and an opponent (mu'tarid). The question is posed by one to move the other from his position.
2
Parse the four types
The author lists four Q&A types: (1) about the madhhab (position), (2) about the dalil (proof), (3) about the wajh dalalat al-dalil (aspect of indication), (4) objection and refutation. Note how each type escalates from the position itself to the proof, its indication, and finally to critical attack.
Type 4 is the most complex and contains multiple sub-forms.
3
Catalog the forms of objection
Under type 4, learn the seven forms: istifsar (inquiry), man' (denial of premise), naqd (counterexample), mu'arada (counter-proof), qalb (inversion), ilzam (forcing contradiction), and qawl bi'l-mujib (conceding premise but denying conclusion). Each is a distinct dialectical move.
These forms are the weapons of the opponent.
4
Apply to a concrete example
Take a debate on qiyas: the proponent says 'Wine is forbidden because it intoxicates; nabidh also intoxicates, so it is forbidden.' The opponent might say 'Man'' (denying nabidh intoxicates) or 'Naqd' (applying the 'illa would also forbid water, which is false). Classify these responses under type 4.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
16 - يقوم الجدل في كل مراحله وفي كلتا طريقتي المتقدمين والمتأخرين على السؤال والجواب المتبادلين بين المستدل والمعترض والسؤال في الجدل كما عرفه ابن عقيل هو الذي يقصد به نقل الخصم عن مذهبه بطريق المحاجة»، أما الجواب فهو كما عرفه الجويني الخبر المضمن بمعنى السؤال .. والسؤال والجواب في الجدل الأصولي أربعة أنواع : السؤال عن المذهب والجواب عنه، السؤال عن الدليل والجواب عنه السؤال عن وجه دلالة الدليل والجواب عنه، السؤال على وجه الاعتراض والقدح والجواب عنه، وهذا النوع الأخير يرد بصور متعددة، منها: الاستفسار المنع النقض المعارضة القلب، الإلزام، القول بالموجب.
16 - Jadal in all its stages and in both methods of the early and later scholars is based on the question and answer exchanged between the proponent (mustadill) and the opponent (mu'tarid). The question in jadal, as defined by Ibn Aqil, is 'that by which one intends to move the opponent from his view by way of argumentation.' As for the answer, it is, as defined by al-Juwayni, 'the statement that includes the meaning of the question.' The question and answer in jadal of usul are of four types: (1) the question about the view (madhhab) and the answer about it; (2) the question about the proof (dalil) and the answer about it; (3) the question about the aspect of indication of the proof (wajh dalalat al-dalil) and the answer about it; (4) the question by way of objection and refutation (i'tirad wa qadh) and the answer about it. This last type occurs in multiple forms, including: istifsar (inquiry), man' (prevention), naqd (refutation), mu'arada (counter-argument), qalb (inversion), ilzam (obligation), and qawl bi'l-mujib (conceding the premise).
Source pages p. 498

Commentary
This passage explains the fundamental structure of jadal as an exchange of question and answer between the proponent (mustadill) and the opponent (mu'tarid). The author cites definitions: Ibn Aqil defines the question as that which aims to move the opponent from his position through argumentation; al-Juwayni defines the answer as a statement that contains the meaning of the question. The author then enumerates four types of question-answer pairs: (1) about the madhhab (the opponent's position), (2) about the dalil (proof), (3) about the wajh dalalat al-dalil (the aspect of indication of the proof), and (4) the objection (i'tirad wa qadh) and its answer. The fourth type has many forms: istifsar (inquiry), man' (prevention of the proof), naqd (refutation by counterexample), mu'arada (counter-argument by presenting a conflicting proof), qalb (inversion, i.e., turning the argument against the proponent), ilzam (forcing a contradiction), and qawl bi'l-mujib (conceding the premise but denying the conclusion). For example, in a debate on qiyas, an opponent might say 'Man' (I deny that the 'illa is present) or 'Naqd' (Your proof fails because it would also forbid water).

Key Takeaway
Jadal is structured around four types of question-answer pairs, with the fourth type encompassing diverse forms of objection.

Inqita' – Breakdown in Jadal and Its Forms
Author defines inqita' and enumerates its seven forms.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the seven forms of inqita' (breakdown) in jadal and distinguish between breakdown of the proponent vs. the opponent.

Core Idea
Inqita' is the debater's incapacity to continue — knowing its forms reveals when a debate has ended in defeat.

Just as jadal has rules for starting, it also has rules for ending. The formal end of a debate occurs when one party can no longer defend their position — this is called 'inqita''. The author lists seven specific forms of breakdown, each indicating a different way a debater fails. Understanding these forms helps you judge when a debate has been resolved or when a disputant has lost their ground.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definition of inqita'
Find al-Baji's definition in the Arabic: 'ajz ahad al-mutanazirayn 'an tashih qawlihi' (the inability of one of the two disputants to correct his statement). Note that it is about inability to correct their own statement, not just to respond.
2
Distinguish the two sides of breakdown
The author says inqita' can be from the mustadill (proponent, who is answering objections) or from the sa'il (opponent/questioner) or both. Understand the difference: a proponent breaks down when he cannot defend his proof; an opponent breaks down when he cannot sustain his objection.
3
List the seven forms of breakdown
Scan the Arabic for the seven forms: mukabara (obstinacy), munaqadat al-madhhab (contradicting one's own school), mushaghaha (quarrelsomeness), istifsar (excessive questioning), ruju' ila al-tasli (resort to surrender), jahd al-madhhab (denying one's own position), musaba (using insults). Write them down.
The last term 'musaba' may be 'musaba' (insults) – OCR suggests this.
4
Analyze each form as a sign of incapacity
For each form, ask: how does this action (e.g., insulting) show that the debater cannot continue properly? For instance, mukabara is refusing to accept clear evidence, which prevents rational progress.
5
Apply the concept to a realistic scenario
Imagine a debate about the ruling of a financial contract. If the opponent starts asking irrelevant questions repeatedly (istifsar) to avoid addressing the proof, that is a breakdown by excessive questioning. Identify which party is suffering breakdown in given scenarios.

17 - وكما أن للجدل بداية فإن له نهاية وهذه النهاية تحصل بعجز المتجادلين عن الاستمرار ويسمى هذا انقطاعا، وقد عرفه الباجي بأنه «عجز أحد المتناظرين عن تصحيح قوله . ...»، وهذا الانقطاع له صور كثيرة، وهو إما أن يكون انقطاعا من المستدل وهو المسئول الذي توخه الاعتراضات إلى دعاواه وأدلته ، أو يكون انقطاعًا من السائل وهو المعترض، أو يحدث هذا الانقطاع من الطرفين، وقد تناولت بالتفصيل والتحليل مجموعة من صور الانقطاع، وهي : الانقطاع بالمكابرة الانقطاع بمناقضة المذهب الانقطاع بالمشاغبة، الانقطاع بالاستفسار الانقطاع بالرجوع إلى التسلي، الانقطاع بجحد المذهب، الانقطاع بالمسببة .
17 - Just as jadal has a beginning, it also has an end, and this end occurs through the inability of the debaters to continue, and this is called inqita' (breakdown). Al-Baji defined it as 'the inability of one of the two disputants to correct his statement.' This breakdown has many forms. It may be a breakdown from the side of the proponent (mustadill), who is the one subjected to objections against his claims and proofs; or it may be a breakdown from the side of the questioner (sa'il), who is the opponent (mu'tarid); or this breakdown may occur from both sides. I have treated in detail and analyzed a set of forms of breakdown, which are: breakdown by obstinacy (mukabara), breakdown by contradiction of the madhhab, breakdown by quarreling (mushaghaha), breakdown by asking too many questions (istifsar), breakdown by resorting to surrender (ruju' ila al-tasli), breakdown by denying the madhhab (jahd al-madhhab), and breakdown by abusing (musaba).
Source pages p. 498

Commentary
This passage discusses the end of jadal, called inqita' (breakdown), which occurs when one or both debaters become unable to continue. The author cites al-Baji's definition: inability of one disputant to correct his statement. The breakdown can happen to the proponent (mustadill) who is defending his position, or to the opponent (mu'tarid) who is objecting, or both. The author lists seven forms of breakdown: (1) mukabara - stubbornly refusing to accept evidence; (2) munaqadat al-madhhab - contradicting one's own school; (3) mushaghaha - being argumentative or quarrelsome; (4) istifsar - excessive questioning that leads to stalling; (5) ruju' ila al-tasli - resorting to surrender, i.e., giving up; (6) jahd al-madhhab - denying one's own stated view; (7) musaba - resorting to insults. Each of these indicates that the debater cannot continue properly and thus is considered defeated. For example, if an opponent in a usul debate starts insulting the proponent instead of providing arguments, that is a breakdown by musaba.

Intiqal: When Transition Becomes Breakdown in Debate
Author defines intiqal as moving to another argument without finishing, distinguishing praiseworthy and blameworthy transition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish praiseworthy from blameworthy transition in a jadal or munazara debate, and identify the five forms of blameworthy intiqal.

Core Idea
Not every transition is a breakdown; only blameworthy transition signals inability.

In the heat of a munazara, a debater may feel the urge to shift course. But when does that shift signal defeat? This passage from Jadal al-Usuliyyin defines intiqal (transition) and lays out the crucial distinction between praiseworthy and blameworthy shifts. Understanding this rule is key to reading debates fairly: a clever move to a stronger argument is allowed, while fleeing from an incomplete argument is a sign of breakdown.

Guided Path
1
Grasp the definition of intiqal
Intiqal is when a debater leaves their current proof or answer before finishing it and moves to something else. The author says this is a clear form of breakdown unless the transition is praiseworthy.
2
Distinguish praiseworthy from blameworthy
Praiseworthy transition (intiqal mahmud) is allowed and does not count as breakdown — e.g., moving to a stronger argument or conceding a valid point. Blameworthy transition (intiqal madhmum) is considered breakdown because it shows the debater's inability to continue.
The author does not give examples of praiseworthy transition here, but the context suggests it is not a sign of defeat.
3
Identify the five forms of blameworthy transition
The author lists five forms: (1) from one proof to another, (2) from one madhhab/position to another, (3) from one answer to another, (4) from one tarjih (preference) to another, (5) from one objection to another. In each case, the debater abandons an incomplete line of reasoning.
These forms are not mutually exclusive; a single move might combine, e.g., shifting both argument and position.
4
Apply to a debate scenario
Take a real usul debate: debater A argues that qiyas is valid for a particular ruling. Before fully defending the parity of the 'illah, A suddenly switches to discussing ijma' on the same issue. This is blameworthy transition from one proof to another (dalin to dalil). It indicates A could not sustain the qiyas argument.
If A had finished the qiyas argument and then moved to ijma' for additional support, it would be praiseworthy — not a breakdown.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
18 - من أبرز صور الانقطاع وأشهر أنواعه : الانتقال، أن يترك أحد وهو المتجادلين ما هو فيه من استدلال أو جواب قبل أن يتمه وينتقل منه إلى غيره، فيكون ذلك دليلا على عجزه وانقطاعه عما كان فيه ؛ وهو إما أن يكون انتقالا محمودًا فلا يكون انقطاعا، أو يكون انتقالاً مذموما فيعتبر انقطاعا، وهذا النوع يرد بعدة صور : انتقال من دليل إلى آخر انتقال من مذهب إلى مذهب من جواب إلى جواب انتقال من ترجيح إلى ترجيح انتقال من اعتراض إلى غيره.
18 - One of the most prominent and famous forms of breakdown is intiqal (transition): that one of the two debaters leaves what he is engaged in, whether it is a proof (istidlal) or an answer, before completing it, and moves from it to something else. This is evidence of his inability and breakdown from what he was in. This transition is either praiseworthy (mahmud) and then it is not a breakdown, or blameworthy (madhmum) and then it is considered a breakdown. This type (blameworthy) occurs in several forms: transition from one proof to another, transition from one madhhab to another, transition from one answer to another, transition from one tarjih (preference) to another, transition from one objection to another.
Source pages p. 499

Commentary
This passage discusses intiqal (transition) as a well-known form of inqita' (breakdown). Intiqal occurs when a debater abandons his current argument or response before completing it and moves to something else. This indicates his inability to maintain his position. However, the author distinguishes between praiseworthy transition (intiqal mahmud), which is not considered a breakdown, and blameworthy transition (intiqal madhmum), which is a breakdown. The praiseworthy transition might be when the debater moves to a stronger argument or acknowledges a valid point. The blameworthy transition is essentially fleeing from the argument. The author lists five forms of blameworthy transition: (1) moving from one proof to another, (2) moving from one madhhab to another (changing position), (3) moving from one answer to another, (4) moving from one tarjih to another, and (5) moving from one objection to another. For example, if a debater in a usul debate starts arguing about qiyas and then suddenly shifts to a discussion of ijma' without finishing the qiyas argument, that is blameworthy transition.

Key Takeaway
Intiqal is considered a form of inqita' (breakdown) only when the transition is blameworthy, i.e., abandoning an incomplete argument due to inability; praiseworthy transitions are allowed and do not signal breakdown.

Outcomes of Jadal in Usul al-Fiqh
Author enumerates the scientific outcomes that jadal typically leads to in usul al-fiqh.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to enumerate and explain the ten scientific outcomes of jadal in usul al-fiqh as identified by the author.

Core Idea
Jadal in usul al-fiqh is a tool for truth-seeking, not mere polemics.

This passage lists ten constructive outcomes that jadal (dialectical debate) typically produces in usul al-fiqh. The author, based on his investigation, presents these outcomes as the fruits of disciplined scholarly debate. Understanding these outcomes will help you see jadal as a rigorous method for advancing knowledge, clarifying disputes, and refining legal theory.

Guided Path
1
Identify the overarching claim
Read the first sentence: 'Usually, jadal in usul issues ends with a scientific result of some kind.' This sets the tone that jadal is not chaotic but productive. Note the author's confidence from his personal investigation.
2
Extract each outcome from the enumeration
The passage lists ten outcomes separated by commas. List them: (1) arriving at truth, (2) correcting attributed statements, (3) correcting weak statements, (4) establishing usul principles, (5) explaining causes of disagreement, (6) defining dispute scope and reconciling, (7) refining proofs, (8) settling pseudo-disputes, (9) providing detailed analysis, (10) uncovering historical origins.
Note the use of 'talks' (typically leads to) indicating these are common results.
3
Classify outcomes by function
Group outcomes: some focus on truth (1,4,6,8), some on textual/authority correction (2,3), some on methodology (5,7,9), and some on historical context (10). This shows jadal operates on multiple levels—logical, historical, and textual.
This classification helps internalize the breadth of jadal's benefits.
4
Relate outcomes to a concrete usul debate
For example, the debate on whether qiyas is valid in the presence of a nass (text). This debate often leads to outcome 7 (refining proofs) and outcome 4 (establishing principles). Think of how such a debate clarifies the conditions of qiyas.
This connects the abstract list to real scholarly practice.
5
Assess the epistemological value of jadal
The list shows jadal is a means of tahqeeq (verification) and tahrir (precise formulation). It corrects errors, refines definitions, and uncovers assumptions. This reframes jadal from mere argumentation to a tool of scientific inquiry.
This step helps you appreciate the depth of the usul tradition.

19 - عادة ما ينتهي الجدل في المسائل الأصولية إلى نتيجة علمية من نوع ما، وقد تبين لي من استقراء هذه النتائج، أن الجدل الأصولي عادة ما يفضي إلى: الوصول إلى الحق في المسألة المختلف فيها تصحيح الأقوال المنسوبة إلى الأئمة والعلماء، تصحيح الأقوال الظاهر ضعفها التوصل إلى تقرير القواعد الأصولية، بيان أسباب الاختلاف، تحرير موضع النزاع والجمع بين الآراء ما أمكن، تنقيح الأدلة التي يجري الاستدلال بها تنقيحًا علميًا، حسم النزاع في المسائل التي لم يقع الاختلاف فيها حقيقة التفصيل في المسائل المختلف فيها وعدم الحكم عليها جملة الكشف عن الأصول التاريخية لنشأة بعض المسائل والآراء فيها.
19 - Usually, jadal in usul issues ends with a scientific result of some kind, and it has become clear to me from investigating these results that jadal of usul typically leads to: arriving at the truth in the disputed issue; correcting the statements attributed to the imams and scholars; correcting statements that appear weak; reaching the establishment of usul principles; explaining the causes of disagreement; defining the scope of the dispute and reconciling opinions as much as possible; scientifically refining the proofs that are used for inference; settling disputes in issues where there is no genuine disagreement; providing detail in disputed issues and not ruling on them summarily; uncovering the historical origins of the emergence of some issues and opinions in them.
Source pages p. 499

Commentary
This passage enumerates the positive outcomes of jadal in usul al-fiqh. The author states that jadal usually ends with a scientific result. From his investigation, he lists ten outcomes: (1) reaching the truth in the disputed matter; (2) correcting statements wrongly attributed to scholars; (3) correcting statements that appear weak but might be valid; (4) establishing usul principles through debate; (5) explaining the reasons for disagreement; (6) defining the exact point of contention and reconciling views when possible; (7) refining proofs used in inference; (8) settling pseudo-disputes where there is no real difference; (9) providing detailed analysis rather than blanket judgments; (10) uncovering historical origins of issues and opinions. These outcomes show that jadal is not just about winning but about advancing knowledge. For example, debates on whether qiyas is valid in the presence of a text (nass) often lead to refining the definition of qiyas and its conditions.

Key Takeaway
Jadal in usul al-fiqh serves as a systematic method to reach truth, refine principles, correct attributions, and clarify disagreements—it is fundamentally constructive.

Types of Interruption and the Concept of Transition in Jadal
Author lists types of inqita' and explains the prominent subtype of intiqal with its varieties.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the seven forms of interruption listed in the text and distinguish between praiseworthy and blameworthy transitions in dialectical debate.

Core Idea
Not all transitions are defeats; strategic shifts are praiseworthy.

In classical Islamic disputation (jadal), not every breakdown is a failure. The author distinguishes between interruption (inqita') as defeat and transition (intiqal) as a specific type of interruption that can be either praiseworthy or blameworthy. This lesson unpacks the taxonomy.

Guided Path
1
List the seven forms of interruption
Scan the Arabic for the enumerated types: المكابرة, مناقضة المذهب, المشاغبة, الاستفسار, الرجوع إلى التسلي, جحد المذهب, المسبة. Match each to its English translation.
Note the unclear term 'التسلي' which may be a variant of 'التسليم'.
2
Define transition (intiqal)
Locate the definition: 'أن يترك أحد وهو المتجادلين ما هو فيه من استدلال أو جواب قبل أن يتمه وينتقل منه إلى غيره' — leaving an argument or response before completion and moving to something else.
3
Differentiate praiseworthy vs blameworthy transitions
The text says a transition is either محمود (praiseworthy) or مذموم (blameworthy). The praiseworthy one is not considered interruption, while the blameworthy is.
A praiseworthy transition could be moving to a stronger evidence.
4
Identify the subtypes of blameworthy transition
The text lists five: انتقال من دليل إلى آخر, من مذهب إلى مذهب, من جواب إلى جواب, من ترجيح إلى ترجيح, من اعتراض إلى غيره.
Each represents a different move the disputant makes to evade the current point.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الطرفين، وقد تناولت بالتفصيل والتحليل مجموعة من صور الانقطاع، وهي : الانقطاع بالمكابرة الانقطاع بمناقضة المذهب الانقطاع بالمشاغبة، الانقطاع بالاستفسار الانقطاع بالرجوع إلى التسلي، الانقطاع بجحد المذهب، الانقطاع بالمسبة 18 من أبرز صور الانقطاع وأشهر أنواعه : الانتقال، أن يترك أحد وهو المتجادلين ما هو فيه من استدلال أو جواب قبل أن يتمه وينتقل منه إلى غيره، فيكون ذلك دليلا على عجزه وانقطاعه عما كان فيه ؛ وهو إما أن يكون انتقالا محمودًا فلا يكون انقطاعا، أو يكون انتقالاً مذموما فيعتبر انقطاعا، وهذا النوع يرد بعدة صور : انتقال من دليل إلى آخر انتقال من مذهب إلى مذهب من جواب إلى جواب انتقال من ترجيح إلى ترجيح انتقال من اعتراض إلى غيره .
The two sides, and it has treated in detail and analysis a set of forms of interruption, namely: interruption by obstinacy, interruption by contradicting the school, interruption by clamor, interruption by inquiry, interruption by resorting to [؟], interruption by denying the school, interruption by insult. 18. Among the most prominent forms of interruption and its most famous types is transition: that one of the two disputants leaves what he is engaged in of argument or response before completing it and transitions from it to something else, and that becomes evidence of his inability and his interruption from what he was in; and it is either a praiseworthy transition in which case it is not interruption, or a blameworthy transition in which case it is considered interruption. And this type comes in several forms: transition from one evidence to another, transition from one school to another, transition from one response to another, transition from one preference to another, transition from one objection to something else.
Source pages p. 499

Commentary
This passage defines the concept of interruption (inqita') in jadal and then focuses on the most prominent type: transition (intiqal). The author first enumerates seven forms of interruption: by obstinacy, contradicting the madhhab, clamor, inquiry, resorting to [؟], denial of the madhhab, and insult. Then, in point 18, the author explains that transition occurs when a disputant abandons his argument or response before completion and moves to something else, indicating his inability. Importantly, not all transitions are defeats; a praiseworthy transition (e.g., moving to a stronger evidence) does not count as interruption, while a blameworthy one does. The author then lists several subtypes of blameworthy transition: moving from one evidence to another, from one madhhab to another, from one answer to another, from one preference to another, and from one objection to another.

Key Terms
الانقطاع
الانتقال

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) Interruption by obstinacy
B) Interruption by silence
C) Interruption by clamor

Key Takeaway
Transition (intiqal) is the most prominent form of interruption, but only blameworthy transitions constitute defeat.

The Constructive Outcomes of Usuli Jadal
Author lists the scientific outcomes resulting from usuli jadal.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and categorize the ten scientific outcomes that usuli jadal typically yields.

Core Idea
Jadal is not mere polemic: it produces real scientific advances.

Many view dialectical debate as a fight for victory, but in the Islamic tradition jadal serves deeper scientific ends. This passage from a work on usuli jadal lists the concrete results that emerge from principled debate. Understanding these outcomes transforms how you approach scholarly disagreement.

Guided Path
1
Read the list of outcomes
Read the Arabic passage and translation carefully. Identify each distinct outcome the author enumerates. There are ten, separated by commas or 'و'.
2
Categorize the outcomes
Group the outcomes into themes: truth-seeking (e.g., reaching truth, clarifying causes), correction (e.g., correcting statements, correcting weak opinions), principle development (e.g., establishing usuli principles, refining evidences), and meta-analysis (e.g., delineating dispute, detailing issues, historical origins).
Some outcomes may fit multiple categories.
3
Reflect on the debater's mindset
Contrast these outcomes with common goals in debate (winning, silencing). How does reorienting to these outcomes change the practice of jadal?
4
Apply to a known usuli dispute
Recall a famous usuli disagreement (e.g., whether qiyas is valid in the presence of a textual cause). Identify which of these outcomes could result from that debate. For example, the debate may 'establish usuli principles' or 'clarify causes of disagreement'.
Use your knowledge of usul al-fiqh.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
عادة ما ينتهي الجدل في المسائل الأصولية إلى نتيجة علمية من نوع ما، وقد تبين لي من استقراء هذه النتائج، أن الجدل الأصولي عادة ما يفضي إلى: الوصول إلى الحق في المسألة المختلف فيها تصحيح الأقوال المنسوبة إلى الأئمة والعلماء، تصحيح الأقوال الظاهر ضعفها التوصل إلى تقرير القواعد الأصولية، بيان أسباب الاختلاف، تحرير موضع النزاع والجمع بين الآراء ما أمكن، تنقيح الأدلة التي يجري الاستدلال بها تنقيحًا علميًا، حسم النزاع في المسائل التي لم يقع الاختلاف فيها حقيقة التفصيل في المسائل المختلف فيها وعدم الحكم عليها جملة الكشف عن الأصول التاريخية لنشأة بعض المسائل والآراء فيها .
Usually jadal in usuli matters ends with a scientific result of some kind, and it has become clear to me from induction of these results that usuli jadal typically leads to: reaching the truth in the disputed issue, correcting statements attributed to imams and scholars, correcting statements that appear weak, arriving at the establishment of usuli principles, clarifying the causes of disagreement, liberating the point of dispute and reconciling opinions as much as possible, refining the evidences used for inference in a scientific manner, settling disputes in issues that have not actually been differed upon, detailing the issues that are disagreed upon and not judging them summarily, and uncovering the historical origins of the emergence of some issues and opinions therein.
Source pages p. 499

Commentary
This passage lists the positive outcomes of jadal in the field of usul al-fiqh. The author states that jadal usually leads to a scientific result, and through induction, he identified several results: first, reaching the truth in disputed matters; second, correcting statements attributed to imams and scholars; third, correcting statements that appear weak; fourth, establishing usuli principles; fifth, clarifying causes of disagreement; sixth, precisely delineating the point of dispute and reconciling views where possible; seventh, scientifically refining the evidences used; eighth, settling disputes in issues that are not genuinely disputed; ninth, detailing issues rather than judging them summarily; and tenth, uncovering historical origins of issues and opinions. The emphasis is that jadal is not mere polemic but a constructive scholarly tool.

النتائج العلمية
القواعد الأصولية
تنقيح الأدلة

The Four Categories of Adab al-Jadal
Author outlines the unique ethical norms of jadal, covering character, relationship, subject, and style.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify any adab of jadal into one of the four categories: personality, relationship, subject, or style.

Core Idea
Jadal's unique ethics are not just politeness but a systematic framework for truth-seeking.

In usuli jadal, the goal is not to win but to manifest the truth. The author presents a structured set of ethical norms that govern this process. These adab are divided into four categories: those related to the disputant's character, the relationship between debaters, the subject under discussion, and the style of argumentation. Understanding these categories helps you self-regulate and evaluate debates.

Guided Path
1
Identify the four categories
Read the Arabic passage and note the four headings: personality (شخصية المجادل), relationship (العلاقة بين المتجادلين), subject (موضوع الجدل), and style (أسلوب الجدل). These are the containers for all subsequent rules.
2
Extract the ethics under each category
For each category, list the specific adab mentioned. For personality: intention, humility, flexibility, etc. For relationship: respect, cooperation, patience, honesty, etc. For subject: knowledge, problem status, precision. For style: economy, strongest evidence, verification, politeness, sound logic, avoidance of fallacies.
Notice that some items appear in multiple categories? The author keeps them separate.
3
Compare with general Islamic adab
Reflect on how these specific adab differ from general etiquette. For example, 'abandoning fanaticism' is not just polite but essential for objective inquiry. 'Not holding the opponent accountable for slips he does not intend' shows a legal-ethical sensitivity unique to debate.
This comparison helps you see the specialized nature of jadal ethics.
4
Apply to a hypothetical scenario
Take a scenario: two scholars debating the ruling on a new financial contract. Determine which adab from each category would apply. For instance, subject adab require the problem be precisely defined; style adab require avoiding logical fallacies.
This bridges theory to practice.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الجدل الأصولي منهج علمي رصين يبتغى به أساسا الوصول إلى الحق والتعاون لإظهاره، ولذلك فهو لا يتوفر فحسب على الأسس والطريقة وإنما يمتاز أيضًا بآدابه الخاصة الفريدة التي يطلب من كل مجادل أن يتحلى بها، وهذه الآداب إما أن تتعلق بشخصية المجادل، أو بالعلاقة بين المتجادلين، أو بموضوع الجدل، أو بأسلوب الجدل. 21 - فأما الآداب التي تتعلق بشخصية المجادل فهي : القصد إلى إظهار الحق والتقرب به إلى الله، التواضع المرونة الفكرية والاستعداد لتقبل الحق عند ظهوره، التمييز بين مراتب من يجادلهم، عدم التسامح مع المخالف فيما يتعلق بالحق، التيقظ لكلام المخالف وتفهمه جيدا التخلي عن التعصب لوجهة النظر السابقة في الموضوع. 22 - وأما الآداب التي تتعلق بالعلاقة بين المتجادلين، فهي : احترام بعضهما البعض وعدم استصغار أحدهما للآخر، الرغبة المشتركة في تبادل الرأي والتعاون لإظهار الحق، توفر الحرية الفكرية لكلا الطرفين، تكيف المتحدث مع المستمع، عدم مؤاخذة المخالف فيما يزل به ولا يقصده، تنبيه كل منهما الآخر إلى ما يقع في كلامه من متناقضات، صبر كل منهما على الآخر حتى يتم كلامه، صدق كل منهما في ما يحكيه من مذهب الآخر. 23 - أما موضوع الجدل، فإن الآداب التي تتعلق به هي: المعرفة بموضوع الجدل أن يكون هذا الموضوع محل إشكال أن تكون مشكلته المتنازع حولها محددة بدقة . 24 - وفيما يتعلق أخيرا بأسلوب الجدل، فإن له هو الآخر آدابه الخاصة التي تتعلق به، وهي: الإلتزام بإيراد ما يحتاج إليه في الجدل فقط، التمسك بأقوى الأدلة ضرورة التثبت في الكلام التقيد بالقول المهذب، التزام الطرق المنطقية السليمة في الجدل التحرز من استعمال الحيل والمغالطات.
Usuli jadal is a solid scientific method primarily seeking to reach the truth and cooperate to manifest it, and therefore it does not merely possess foundations and method but is also distinguished by its own unique ethics that every disputant is required to adopt. These ethics either relate to the personality of the disputant, or to the relationship between the disputants, or to the subject of the debate, or to the style of the debate. 21. As for the ethics related to the personality of the disputant, they are: intending to manifest the truth and draw near to Allah through it, humility, intellectual flexibility and readiness to accept the truth when it appears, distinguishing the ranks of those he debates, not compromising with the opponent regarding the truth, being alert to the opponent's words and understanding them well, abandoning fanaticism for a previous viewpoint on the subject. 22. As for the ethics related to the relationship between the disputants, they are: respecting each other and not belittling one another, a shared desire to exchange opinions and cooperate to manifest the truth, providing intellectual freedom for both parties, the speaker adapting to the listener, not holding the opponent accountable for slips he does not intend, each one alerting the other to contradictions in his speech, each one being patient with the other until he completes his speech, each one being truthful in what he reports of the other's madhhab. 23. As for the subject of the debate, the ethics related to it are: knowledge of the subject of debate, that this subject be problematic, that its disputed problematic be precisely defined. 24. Finally, regarding the style of debate, it too has its own ethics: committing only to bring what is needed in the debate, adhering to the strongest evidences, the necessity of verification in speech, adhering to polite speech, committing to sound logical methods in debate, avoiding the use of tricks and fallacies.
Source pages p. 500

Commentary
This passage establishes the ethical framework for jadal. The author begins by affirming that usuli jadal is a rigorous scientific method aimed at truth, not mere argumentation. It has unique adab (ethics) divided into four categories. First, ethics related to the disputant's character: sincerity seeking Allah's pleasure, humility, intellectual openness, recognizing the opponent's level, uncompromising on truth, attentiveness to the opponent's words, and abandoning prejudice. Second, ethics of the relationship: mutual respect, cooperation, intellectual freedom, adaptation, forgiveness for unintended slips, mutual correction of contradictions, patience, and honesty in reporting the opponent's view. Third, ethics of the subject: the debater must know the subject well, ensure it is genuinely problematic, and precisely define the point of contention. Fourth, ethics of style: economy of argument, using strongest proofs, careful speech, politeness, sound logic, and avoiding fallacies and tricks.

Key Terms
الآداب
شخصية المجادل
العلاقة بين المتجادلين
موضوع الجدل
أسلوب الجدل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Humility
B) Patience
C) Committing to sound logical methods
D) Defining the subject precisely

Key Takeaway
The adab of jadal are systematically organized into four categories—character, relationship, subject, and style—each with specific norms that ensure debate remains truth-seeking and constructive.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

(c) A debater uses personal insults. (d) A debater insists on his opinion even after clear evidence. (e) A debater misrepresents his opponent's view.
?
